2. 
Key 
policy 
and 
institutional 
reforms 
supported 
by 
the 
project: 
The 
project 
will 
contribute 
to two 
areas 
of 
institutional 
reforms. 
These 
policy 
reforms 
are 
already 
being 
supported 
through 
the 
Programmatic 
Social 
Reform 
Loans 
(PSRLI 
and 
PSRLIQ, and 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
to 
their 
implementation: 
Human 
Resources 
policy. 
The Program 
will 
support 
implementation 
of 
a 
far-reaching, 
systemic 
reform 
designed 
to 
improve 
the 
quality 
of 
teachers and 
teaching 
in 
Peru 
by 
addressing 
in 
a 
synergetic 
manner the 
issues 
discussed 
earlier. 
-20- 
Page  27
The 
policy 
was 
developed 
by 
a team 
of 
national 
consultants, 
who 
reviewed 
all 
its 
components 
in 
light 
of 
their 
likely 
impact 
on 
student 
learning, 
especially 
for 
students 
from 
disadvantaged 
backgrounds, 
and 
with 
a 
view 
to 
creating virtuous 
circles 
in 
which 
capacity-building 
and 
incentives 
point 
in 
the 
same 
direction 
and 
reinforce 
each 
other. 
The central piece 
of 
the 
new 
policy 
would 
be 
a 
set 
of 
teaching 
standards 
(10 
initially, 
to 
be 
elaborated 
upon 
by 
level, discipline, 
etc.) 
reflecting national 
and 
international 
research 
findings 
about 
what 
makes 
a 
good 
teacher 
in 
the 
Peruvian context. 
These  standards 
which 
describe 
the  type 
of 
competencies 
(knowledge, 
skills, 
attitudes, 
values) 
expected 
from 
teachers 
at 
different 
levels 
of 
performance 
(novice, 
professional, 
expert, unsatisfactory), 
were 
developed 
in 
a 
participatory 
manner  and 
broadly 
discussed 
to 
ensure 
relevance 
and 
ownership 
by 
the 
profession. The expectation 
was 
that 
greater 
clarity 
should 
guide  improvements 
in 
practice, 
focus  the 
efforts 
of 
teacher 
trainers, 
and 
facilitate 
performance 
evaluation 
by 
reducing 
the 
degree 
of 
subjectivity 
of 
the 
process 
and 
the 
resulting 
anxiety 
for 
those 
being 
evaluated. 
A 
second 
pillar 
of 
the 
new 
policy 
would 
be a 
new, 
more 
differentiated 
professional 
career 
path 
in 
which 
progression 
would 
reward, 
instead 
of 
the  bureaucratic 
years 
of 
service, 
evidence 
of 
acquisition 
of 
the 
above 
competencies 
at 
growing 
levels 
of 
performance 
and 
the 
taking 
on 
of 
new responsibilities. 
Initially 
participation 
in 
this 
“career 
of 
excellence” 
would 
be 
on 
a 
voluntary 
basis, 
with 
higher pay 
for 
those 
teachers 
willing to 
be 
evaluated 
and 
to 
forego 
unconditional 
job 
stability. 
After 
a 
period 
of 
trial 
and 
refinement 
of 
the 
instruments, 
all 
teachers 
would 
be 
included 
in 
the 
scheme, 
except 
for 
those  near 
retirement. 
The 
proposed 
five-step 
career 
would 
recognize the 
needs 
of 
teachers at 
different 
stages 
of 
their professional 
life, 
featuring 
a 
probatory 
induction 
process 
for 
beginners and mentoring 
of 
young 
or 
struggling 
teachers 
by 
seasoned 
colleagues. 
All 
teachers 
would 
be 
expected 
to 
move 
up 
the 
ladder 
for 
the 
first 
three 
levels 
while promotion 
to 
the 
upper levels 
would 
be 
more 
selective. 
However, 
a 
fast track 
would 
be 
available 
for 
the 
more motivated. 
Linked 
to 
this 
new 
career, 
a 
third 
pillar 
would 
be a 
revised compensation 
schedule 
which 
would 
introduce 
the pay 
raises 
promised 
by 
the 
administration. 
Reflecting 
the 
research 
finding 
that 
both 
the 
level 
and 
structure 
of 
pay  determine 
teacher 
effectiveness, 
the 
schedule 
would 
be  considerably 
streamlined, 
featuring 
a 
basic 
salary 
plus 
bonuses 
for 
ruralhardship 
posting, 
for 
additional 
responsibilities, 
and 
for 
performance (possibly on 
a 
whole-school 
basis). 
A 
recent 
teacher 
survey 
has 
shown that a 
majority 
(60 
percent) support the 
idea 
of 
merit 
pay 
as 
long 
as 
the 
criteria 
are 
clear, support 
is 
provided, 
and 
the 
process 
is 
fair. 
The 
details and 
fiscal 
impact 
are 
still 
being 
worked 
out. 
The 
continuum 
of 
standards 
would 
also 
be the 
linchpin for 
a much-needed 
teacher 
quality 
assurance 
(QA) 
system 
with 
the 
following 
key 
features: 
0 
Accreditation 
ofZSPs. 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
higher 
quality 
of 
teacher 
preparation, over 
the 
first 
two 
phases 
of 
the 
program, 
all 
ISPs 
would 
have 
to 
subject 
themselves 
to 
a process 
of 
renewable accreditation 
by 
a 
specialized Commission 
and 
peers. 
This 
process 
would 
take 
into 
account, 
in 
addition 
to 
the usual 
institutional 
dimensions 
(curriculum, faculty, 
resources), 
the expected 
profile 
of 
beginning 
teachers. 
e 
Teacher 
certification. 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
minimal 
standards 
of 
teacher 
competency 
across 
the 
board, 
only certified 
teachers 
would 
be 
eligible 
for 
appointment 
and 
authorized 
to 
teach. 
Initially, 
a 
temporary 
certification 
would 
be 
issued 
for 
candidates 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
a 
degree 
from 
an 
accredited 
institution 
(university 
or 
ISP)  and 
the passing 
of 
a test 
based 
on 
the teaching 
standards. 
Full 
initial 
certification 
would 
be subject 
to 
the successful 
completion 
of 
a process 
of 
supervised 
induction 
in 
the 
classroom. 
-21 
- 
Page  28
Re-certification. 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
that the 
average 
competency 
level 
of 
the 
entire teaching 
corps rises 
over 
time, 
all 
teachers 
would 
have 
to 
be 
re-certified 
every 
5-7 
years 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
the 
standards. 
It 
is 
expected 
that 
the 
certification 
and 
re-certification 
process 
will 
be voluntary 
during 
the 
initial 
period, 
being 
converted 
to 
compulsory later 
on. 
consistent 
and 
fair 
system 
of 
performance 
evaluation, 
based 
on  the 
standards. 
The 
system 
would 
combine 
formative 
evaluation 
on 
a 
yearly  cycle  to 
improve 
practice 
with 
summative evaluation 
for 
accountability 
and 
promotions. 
It 
would 
capture the many 
facets 
of 
teaching and 
would involve 
different 
stakeholders, 
especially 
school-based 
commissions and 
the sub-regional offices. 
e 
Perjormance  evaluation. 
Considerable 
attention 
was 
paid 
to 
the  design 
of 
a 
transparent, 
Overall,  the 
standards 
based 
policy 
would 
strengthen 
alignment between 
stated 
goals 
and 
classroom 
practice. The 
quality 
of 
the 
intake 
into 
faculties 
of 
education 
would 
be 
expected 
to 
increase 
with 
improved 
compensation and 
(possibly) 
scholarships. 
Accredited 
training 
institutions 
would 
do 
a 
better 
job 
of 
preparing 
teachers, 
whose  competencies 
would 
be 
continuously 
upgraded through 
multiple 
opportunities 
for 
professional 
development, 
tightly linked 
to 
the design 
of 
the 
reform 
and 
the 
needs 
of 
schools. 
The latter 
would 
receive 
heightened support 
but 
would 
be 
held 
accountable 
for 
the 
learning 
results 
of 
pupils. 
In 
line 
with 
the 
govemment 
stated 
priorities, 
the 
incentive 
system 
would 
reward 
deployment 
to rural 
aqd 
hardship 
posts. 
While 
the 
overall 
emphasis 
would 
be on 
growth 
and support, 
sanctions 
for 
persistent 
under-performers 
(from 
ISP 
closure 
to 
teacher 
dismissal) 
would 
give 
the 
system 
credibility. 
The 
MIS 
system 
would 
be upgraded 
to 
facilitate 
monitoring 
and 
promote 
transparency. 
Deconcentration and decentralization 
toward 
school 
autonomy. 
The  GOP 
is 
currently 
addressing 
the 
problem 
of 
having 
a 
highly 
centralized 
government 
which 
is 
inefficient 
and 
bureaucratic. 
The 
President 
of 
the 
Council 
of 
Ministers 
is 
currently 
leading 
the 
process 
of 
drafting 
a 
decentralization 
proposal, 
which 
will 
include 
the 
Education 
Sector, 
to 
be 
submitted 
to 
Congress. 
While 
this 
process 
is 
going 
on, 
MED 
has 
identified 
initial 
steps 
to 
be 
taken 
within 
the 
sector 
that 
will 
contribute 
to 
a 
greater 
focus 
on 
the 
school. 
It 
is 
expected that the proposed actions 
will 
not 
be 
affected 
by 
any 
decentralization 
scheme 
proposed 
in 
the new 
law. 
Restructuring 
of 
the Regional ofices. 
Regional 
offices 
are 
in 
the 
process 
of 
restructuring 
in 
order 
to 
eliminate the various former 
structures 
(USE 
and 
ADE) 
to 
create 
the 
UGEs 
(Unidades 
de 
Gestidn 
Educativa), 
which 
will 
focu; 
on 
local 
supervision 
and support 
to 
schools. 
The 
UGEs 
will 
be 
made 
accountable 
for 
school performance 
within 
their 
jurisdictions. 
In 
addition, 
rural 
school networks 
will 
be 
created 
to 
provide 
closer-to-school 
support 
in 
rural 
areas 
where  schools are 
very 
small 
and 
highly 
dispersed. 
The networks 
will 
provide 
a 
venue 
for 
teachers 
to 
come 
together 
to 
discuss 
their 
problems and 
solutions 
as 
well 
as 
to 
receive training.  They 
will 
also 
provide 
a 
closer-to-school layer 
of 
management, 
facilitating 
contact 
with 
the 
UGEs 
and 
linking 
PEIs 
from 
small 
and dispersed schools 
into 
a 
network-PEI. 
Decentralization toward 
the 
school. 
MED 
has 
a 
clear 
vision 
toward 
school 
autonomy. 
However, 
it 
is 
keenly 
aware 
that reaching 
this 
point 
requires capacity 
building 
and 
changes 
in 
the 
legal 
framework. 
Autonomy 
clearly 
requires capacity 
building 
development 
to 
ensure 
that the school 
and 
community 
can 
handle 
this 
new role. 
The 
first 
steps 
in 
this 
direction 
have 
been taken 
in 
the creation 
of 
compulsory 
school 
councils 
which 
are 
expected 
to 
participate 
in 
the 
development, 
implementation 
and evaluation 
of 
the 
Institutional 
Education Plan 
(PEI), 
evaluate the 
use 
of 
school 
and 
Parent Association 
funds 
and 
link 
the 
community 
to 
school activities. 
School councils 
are 
integrated 
by 
the school director 
(who 
presides 
it), 
sub-directors 
(if 
the 
position 
exists 
in 
the 
school), 
two 
teachers, 
two 
students, 
and 
two 
representatives 
of 
the 
parents. 
In 
the 
future, 
and subject 
to 
legal 
reform, 
it 
is 
expected 
that school councils 
will 
manage 
- 
22 
- 
Page  29
funds 
and, 
in 
the 
long 
term, 
be 
responsible 
for 
hiring 
and 
paying 
teachers. 
As 
a 
first 
step, 
this 
Project 
includes the School Development 
Fund 
which 
will 
transfer money 
to 
the 
school councils 
to 
finance key 
activities 
in 
the 
PEI 
that 
will 
lead 
to 
improved 
results 
at 
the 
school level. 
3. 
Benefits and 
target 
population: 
I 
The Program 
is 
targeted 
to rural 
children 
of 
all 
school 
ages 
through 
increased coverage and 
quality 
of 
education. 
The 
policy 
changes 
identified 
as 
necessary 
to 
achieve 
the 
Program objectives 
are 
expected 
to 
benefit 
the 
education 
system 
as 
a whole. 
Expected beneficiaries 
for 
each 
component 
will 
be: 
Expanding 
Access 
(a) 
The 
non-formal 
and 
formal 
preschool education 
program 
would 
benefit 
30,000 
children 
0-2 
years 
old 
in 
rural 
areas, 
including 
a 
large 
proportion 
of 
indigenous 
children 
and 
38,000 
children 
in 
preschool 
programs, 
of 
which 
11,000 
would 
represent 
increased 
coverage. 
(b) 
The distance 
secondary 
education program 
is 
expected 
to 
benefit 
approximately 
5,000 
rural 
students 
in 
116 
schools. 
Improving Quality 
(c) 
The 
continuous 
teacher 
training 
system 
will 
benefit 
directly 
(i) 
1,500 
teacher 
trainers 
in 
public 
sectors 
non-university 
training 
institutions; 
and 
(ii) 
3,800 
teachers 
in 
the 
Project  Area 
working 
in 
multigrade 
schools, and 
15,000 
bilingual 
teachers 
nationwide 
(of 
which 
1,800 
will 
be 
in 
the 
networks and 
will 
benefit 
from 
both 
training 
programs). 
(d) 
330,000 
rural 
children 
in 
the 
Project 
Area 
will 
also 
benefit 
from 
both 
teacher 
training 
and 
from 
the 
provision 
of 
materials, 
as 
these 
factors 
are 
associated 
with 
reduction 
in 
repetition 
and 
desertion 
rates 
and 
with 
improved 
performance 
in 
the 
classroom. 
(e) 
375,000 
indigenous 
children  nationwide 
will 
also 
benefit 
from 
teacher 
training 
in 
intercultural 
bilingual 
education 
and 
from 
the 
provisions 
of 
bilingual 
educational 
materials. 
(f) 
About 
1,900 
classrooms 
in 
poor 
rural 
areas 
will 
benefit 
from 
rehabilitation 
or  substitution 
of 
infrastructure. 
Reform 
of 
Teacher 
Policy and Education 
Management 
(g) 
Increased school 
autonomy and 
networks 
will 
benefit 
3,180 
schools 
by 
allowing 
teachers 
and 
local 
communities 
to 
carry out 
local 
planning 
and 
receiving 
closer and more frequent 
support 
from 
itinerant 
teacher 
support 
groups. 
(h) 
Restructuring 
of 
regional 
offices 
and 
strengthening 
of 
MEDs 
statistical 
system 
will 
contribute 
to 
overall 
greater 
efficiency 
of 
the 
system.

(i)

-23-

activities. 
FONCODES

These are

Project  Coordination. 
MED 
will 
be 
assisted 
by 
a 
PCU 
responsible 
for 
overall

and 
monitoring 
of 
project 
implementation.  The 
PCU 
will 
be 
accountable 
to 
the 
Alta 
Direccidn 
(the 
Minister 
and 
Vice-Ministers) 
and 
will 
report 
to 
the 
Project  Steering  Committee, 
in 
which 
the 
key 
MED 
directorates 
will 
participate. 
Detailed 
procedures 
for 
procurement 
and 
financial 
management 
processes 
will 
be 
specified 
in 
the 
Operations 
Manual. 
Regardless 
of 
size, 
contracting 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
using 
procurement procedures 
and standard 
bidding 
documents 
satisfactory 
to 
the 
Bank. 
The 
PCU 
will 
procure 
goods 
and 
services. 
Operations Manuals. 
The 
project 
will 
use  an 
Operations 
Manual 
covering 
all 
aspects 
of 
project 
implementation, 
including 
but 
not 
limited 
to: 
project 
components, 
organizational 
and

structures, 
supervision 
and

mechanisms; 
targeting and other 
criteria 
for 
resource 
allocation; 
procedures 
for 
the 
formulation 
of 
annual 
implementation 
plans, 
financial,  accounting  and

procedures; 
procurement 
procedures  and  standard 
bidding 
documents; 
structure 
of 
the 
Steering 
Committee 
and 
environmental guidelines 
to 
be 
used 
in 
the

of 
the 
schools. 
The adoption 
of 
an 
Operations 
Manual 
satisfactory 
to 
the 
Bank 
is 
a 
condition 
for 
effectiveness. 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation 
Arrangements. 
Project 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
will 
measure 
not only 
procedures 
but 
also 
results, 
thereby 
allowing 
the 
project 
to 
learn. 
The 
PCU 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
monitoring 
of 
the 
Project 
as 
well 
as 
for 
results 
and

Monitoring 
of 
the coverage 
of 
the 
Project 
interventions 
will 
be 
based 
on 
PCU 
administrative 
data 
and 
statistical 
information 
from 
MEDs 
information 
system. 
Given 
that 
the system 
requires 
further 
strengthening, the 
Project 
will 
finance technical 
assistance 
to 
MEDs 
Statistics 
Unit 
in 
order 
to 
refurbish 
the 
data

and

system, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
GIs. 
Progress 
reports, 
including monitoring 
indicators 
will 
be 
submitted 
by 
the 
PCU 
every six 
months. 
An 
annual 
review 
of 
the 
Project 
will 
be 
carried out 
jointly 
with 
the 
Bank 
based 
on 
these Progress 
reports. 
The 
Bank 
will 
carry out 
at 
least 
two 
technical supervision missions 
per 
year 
in 
close coordination 
with 
MED, 
FONCODES 
and 
MEF. 
Prior 
to 
December 
2005, 
the  Bank, 
in 
close 
collaboration 
with 
MED 
and 
FONCODES 
will 
conduct 
a 
comprehensive 
mid-term 
evaluation 
of 
the 
Project.  The results 
of 
this 
evaluation 
will 
be 
used 
to 
propose 
eventual 
adjustments 
or 
changes 
to 
improve Project 
implementation. 
Evaluation 
of 
the 
Project

and 
impact 
will 
be 
based 
on data 
of 
the 
LSMS 
and

sample-based 
and

The

aim 
at 
both 
assessing 
the

as 
well 
as 
to 
learn 
and

to 
improve project 
implementation.  The evaluation 
studies 
will 
include, 
but 
will 
not 
be 
limited 
to, 
a

an

and 
a 
case-study 
to

impact on 
teacher 
training 
in 
their 
performance 
in 
the 
classroom 
as 
well 
as 
in 
the 
learning 
processes.

D. 
Project Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
An APL 
vs 
a regular 
investment 
project 
The 
Government 
has 
set 
clear 
goals 
for 
educational 
sector 
improvement and 
has 
publicly 
committed 
to 
major 
reforms 
of 
teacher 
policy 
and 
education 
management, 
two 
of 
the 
longest standing constraints 
to 
educational 
progress 
in 
Peru. 
It 
will 
require 
years 
to 
implement 
and consolidate 
these 
ambitious reforms. 
Because 
the 
Government 
has 
established a 
medium-term 
vision 
for 
the 
sector 
and 
is 
directly tackling 
the 
key 
sectorial 
issues 
identified 
in 
previous 
analytical work 
by 
the 
World 
Bank 
and other 
groups, 
an 
APL 
is 
the 
most 
appropriate instrument 
for providing 
sustained 
support 
to 
the Government over the 
reform 
period. 
Because 
of 
the focus on 
providing timely 
assistance 
to 
the 
GOP 
as 
it 
moves 
quickly 
with 
the design 
and 
implementation 
of 
these 
reforms, 
the 
details 
of 
investments 
to 
be 
supported 
under the entire 
project 
cannot be 
fully 
appraised 
at 
this 
point.  The 
APL 
instrument 
provides 
the 
flexibility to pilot 
and evaluate 
proposed 
investments 
(such 
as 
the 
secondary 
distance 
education modalities) 
during 
the 
Phase 
I 
of 
the 
program 
and, 
on 
this 
basis, 
to 
develop 
the 
investment 
priorities 
for 
further 
years, 
which 
a 
regular 
investment program 
would 
not. 
Exclusive 
focus 
on basic 
education 
vs. 
inclusion 
of 
pre-school and 
ECD 
investments 
as 
well. 
The 
inclusion 
of 
pre-school and 
ECD 
investments 
bring 
in 
an 
added 
level 
of 
implementation 
complexity 
to 
an 
already 
complex Project. 
However, the pedagogical and economic benefits stemming 
from 
the 
investment 
on 
early 
years 
have 
been shown 
to 
play 
a 
decisive 
role 
in 
children’s performance 
in 
primary 
school 
and 
later. 
Thus, 
because 
of 
the importance 
that 
ECD 
and pre-school 
exposure 
has 
for 
achieving 
the 
core 
project 
goal 
of 
100 
percent 
primary 
completion 
in 
rural 
areas 
as 
well 
as 
their 
numerous 
other 
benefits, 
it 
was 
decided 
to 
include 
these 
investments 
in 
the 
Program. 
However, 
the 
ECD 
interventions 
are 
being 
introduced 
in 
Phase 
I 
only 
on 
a 
pilot 
basis, 
due 
to 
the 
lack 
of 
significant 
prior 
experience 
in 
the 
rural 
areas. 
Supply 
side 
vs. 
demand 
side 
financing. 
Although 
it 
is 
clear that education supply 
in 
rural 
areas 
is 
constrained 
at 
the 
pre-school 
and 
secondary 
levels, 
primary 
school 
coverage 
is 
quite 
high 
and close 
to 
100 
percent 
of 
rural 
children begin 
first 
grade 
at some 
point. 
The 
significantly 
lower 
completion 
rate 
in 
rural 
areas 
undoubtedly 
stems 
from 
the 
low 
quality 
of 
many 
schools, 
but 
demand 
for 
schooling 
may also 
be constrained 
by 
direct 
costs 
to 
parents 
(for 
uniforms 
and 
materials) 
and 
opportunity 
costs. 
Demand-side 
interventions 
in 
Brazil 
(Bolsa  Escola), 
Mexico 
(Progresa) 
and 
several  other 
Latin 
American countries have 
been 
effective 
in 
increasing schooling 
retention 
rates 
among 
children 
from 
very 
poor 
families. 
It 
was 
agreed 
that 
an 
analysis 
of 
this 
issue 
would 
be 
carried 
out under 
Phase 
I 
in 
order 
to 
include relevant interventions 
during 
the 
second 
and 
third 
phases. 
Traditional 
secondary 
education 
vs 
distance 
education 
Analyses have 
shown that 
traditional 
models 
of 
secondary 
education 
in 
Peru’s 
highly 
dispersed 
rural 
- 
25 
- 
Page  32
areas 
would 
be 
prohibitively 
expensive. 
Although 
the 
use 
of  ICT 
(Information 
Communication 
Technology 
- 
ICT) 
for 
distance education 
offers 
the 
clear 
potential 
for 
a 
more cost-effective 
secondary 
education 
service 
delivery 
in 
rural 
areas, 
it 
is 
not 
yet clear 
which 
of 
several 
alternative models 
is 
most 
appropriate and effective 
in 
the 
Peruvian context. 
The 
program 
will 
support 
the 
piloting 
of 
several 
alternative models 
during 
Phase 
I 
and 
a 
careful 
evaluation 
of 
these, 
to 
guide 
the 
choice 
of 
model or 
models 
to 
be adopted 
in 
the 
subsequent 
program 
phases. 
2.

related 
projects 
financed

and/or

3. 
Lessons 
learned and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
Rural 
education 
has 
been 
particularly 
defying 
when 
trying 
to 
implement actions 
to 
achieve 
equity and 
quality 
objectives. 
Projects 
such 
as 
EDURURAL 
in 
Northeast 
Brazil, 
Colombia’s 
Escuela 
Nueva, 
and 
El 
Salvador’s 
EDUCO, 
among others 
have 
been 
exemplary 
on how 
to 
address 
the specific 
needs 
of 
rural 
education 
in 
a 
diversity 
of 
approaches 
and many 
lessons 
have 
been 
derived 
from 
them 
in 
the design 
of 
this 
Project. 
Bilingual 
education 
has 
proven 
to 
be 
a 
cost-effective 
means 
to 
reduce 
repetition 
and dropout 
rates 
as 
well 
as 
low 
academic 
achievement 
in 
indigenous populations, 
as 
observed 
in 
a 
number 
of 
World 
Bank 
projects 
in 
Mexico, 
Guatemala and 
Bolivia. 
The current 
bilingual 
education 
program 
in 
Peru 
has 
yielded 
valuable 
information 
on 
teacher 
training, 
production 
and 
distribution 
of 
materials 
and 
communication 
with 
the 
community, 
which 
have 
been 
taken 
into 
consideration 
during 
the 
design 
of 
the 
relevant activities 
to 
be 
carried out 
under 
the 
Project. 
Teacher 
training 
has 
been 
object 
of 
renewed 
and 
increasing interest 
as 
the most 
important 
quality 
factor 
for 
school-based 
education improvement. Projects 
like 
the 
Brazilian 
FUNDESCOLA 
and 
Parand, 
the 
Mexican PAREB 
and 
the 
Uruguayan 
MECAEP 
emphasized 
teacher 
training 
in 
a 
way that 
proved 
to 
be 
decisive. 
The 
proposed 
project 
will 
help 
design 
a 
carefully 
conceived “continuous 
teacher 
training 
system” 
with 
immediate 
application 
to 
teacher 
training 
in 
rural 
areas. 
School 
autonomy 
has 
been 
the 
focus 
of 
well-known 
and 
positive 
education reforms 
in 
Minas 
Gerais 
(Brazil),  Chile, 
El 
Salvador and 
Nicaragua 
as 
a 
meaningful 
strategy 
to 
boost 
local 
ownership 
and 
community participation. The 
proposed 
project 
will 
support 
school 
autonomy 
mainly 
by 
the 
development 
of 
the “education 
community” 
concept, 
strengthening 
of 
the school councils and 
the development and 
implementation 
of 
the 
school-centered 
Institutional 
Education Plan 
- 
PEL 
The 
concept 
of 
school 
network 
as 
an 
instrument 
for 
micro-regional 
school association 
and 
planning 
has 
been 
implemented 
in 
Ecuador 
and 
in 
Ceard 
(Brazil) 
with 
the 
Regional Education 
Development 
Centers 
- 
CREDE. 
In 
the 
project preparation period, Peruvian professionals benefited 
from 
Ecuadorian 
assistance 
and 
visited 
the 
Ceard model. 
Both 
cases 
inspired 
the 
design 
of 
the 
proposed school 
network 
structure, 
which 
seeks 
to 
promote closer-to-school 
management 
and 
pedagogical 
initiatives 
in 
rural 
areas. 
Provision 
of 
secondary 
education  through  distance 
leaming, 
with 
support 
from 
information 
and 
communications 
technology, 
has 
been 
carried 
out 
in 
a 
number 
of 
countries, 
including 
Mexico 
and 
Colombia, 
Results 
are 
encouraging showing 
positive 
impacts 
both 
in 
coverage and 
quality 
that 
are 
comparable 
or, 
sometimes, 
better than 
traditional 
secondary 
education. 
This 
project 
also 
builds 
on 
the 
lessons 
learned 
form 
the 
Peru  Primary 
Education 
Quality 
Project, 
particularly 
on 
teacher 
training 
and 
production 
of 
materials. 
This 
Project 
found 
a successful 
mechanism 
for 
the 
provision 
of 
teacher 
training 
through the 
use 
of 
Universities,  ISPs 
and 
NGOs, 
as 
opposed 
to 
internal 
training 
programs. 
Production 
and 
distribution 
of 
materials 
for 
primary 
grades 
in 
Spanish 
was 
also 
successful 
and 
is 
now 
being carried 
out 
regularly 
by 
MED. 
These processes 
will 
continue 
during 
the 
implementation 
of 
this 
project. 
Only 
materials 
targeted 
to 
the 
multigrade 
classrooms 
(including 
self-paced 
materials) 
will 
be produced and 
tested. 
The 
same 
strategy 
will 
be 
used 
during 
this 
project. 
The previous project, 
was 
implemented 
by 
the 
Ministry 
under 
MECEP-PCU 
coordination. 
The 
Project 
-27- 
Page  34
PCU 
staff 
was 
only 
dedicated 
to 
the 
administration 
of 
the 
Project 
funds. 
This 
project 
implementation 
system 
ensures 
process 
ownership 
and 
sustainability. However, 
it 
also 
creates 
lags 
in 
implementation 
mainly 
because 
there 
is 
great 
need 
for 
intra-institutional 
coordination. The 
first 
lesson 
learned 
is 
that 
it 
is 
better 
to 
sacrifice timeliness 
for 
ownership. 
Even 
though 
it 
would 
be more 
practical 
and 
timely 
to 
have a 
separate 
group 
of 
people executing project activities, 
it 
is 
best 
to 
involve 
the 
whole 
Ministry, 
keeping 
the 
responsibility 
under 
the 
directorships. 
The 
proposed project 
would 
also 
be implemented 
by 
the 
Ministry 
while 
intra-institutional 
coordination 
issues 
would 
be 
addressed 
through 
the 
participating 
directorates 
and 
facilitated 
by 
the Steering Committee. 
A 
small-size and 
qualified 
PCU 
was 
able enough 
to 
manage, 
particularly 
for 
the last 
two 
years, 
the 
Primary Education Quality 
Improvement 
Project 
- 
MECEP 
(1996-2001) 
and carry 
it 
out 
to 
successful 
and 
timely 
completion. 
Among 
other 
lessons learned, 
the 
continuity 
of 
committed 
staff 
was 
key 
to 
successful 
implementation. 
Through the preparation 
of 
the Programmatic Social 
Reform 
Loans (PSRL 
I 
and 
PSRL 
II) 
there 
was 
frequent 
contact 
with 
other 
ongoing 
Peruvian projects 
in 
the  social 
areas. 
This 
helped place 
this 
education 
project 
in 
a 
broader 
political 
and 
administrative  framework, 
which 
has 
added 
synergy 
to 
project-supported 
components. 
One 
of 
the 
lessons 
learned 
in 
this 
process 
is 
that the 
Bank 
needs 
to 
pay 
more 
attention 
to 
inter-institutional 
processes 
as 
part 
of 
project 
design 
and 
supervision. 
This 
new 
project 
will 
benefit 
from 
the 
same 
interaction 
with 
other 
Bank 
or 
otherwise social 
projects 
in 
the 
country. 
The 
availability 
of 
a 
well-conducted 
sector 
work 
means 
a 
source 
of 
information 
and 
a 
comparative 
point 
for 
data 
validation. 
This 
role 
was 
played 
by 
"Peruvian 
Education 
at 
a Crossroads 
: 
Challenges and 
Opportunities 
for 
the 
21st 
Century" (Washington, 
DC: 
The 
World 
Bank, 
2001) 
in 
the 
whole 
preparation 
process. 
4. 
Indications 
of 
borrower 
commitment and 
ownership: 
The 
Project strongly 
reflects 
State and 
GOP 
priorities 
put 
forth 
in 
the 
National 
Agreement 
as 
well 
as 
priorities 
which 
have 
been 
stated 
in 
its 
major 
policy 
document 
as 
well 
as 
in 
other specific 
documents 
which 
have already 
been 
published 
or 
are 
in 
the preparation 
process. 
For 
exaqple, 
MED 
prepared the 
"Politica Nacional 
de Lenguas y 
Culturas" (National Policies 
on 
Language and Culture), 
a document 
that 
reflects 
the 
national 
bilingual 
intercultural policy, 
which 
was 
developed under 
wide 
consultation 
with 
stakeholders. 
MED 
is 
also 
finalizing 
its 
document 
on 
rural 
education 
policy 
which 
has 
also been 
prepared 
under 
wide consultation 
with 
stakeholders.  Studies 
and 
workshops 
were 
carried 
out 
by 
MED 
to 
propose 
a 
new teaching 
career 
including 
salary 
scales, 
accreditation 
systems 
for 
teachers 
and 
teacher 
training 
institutions 
and 
teacher 
evaluation criteria. 
These 
policies 
are 
reflected 
in 
the 
Bank's 
Programmatic 
Social 
Reform 
Loans 
(PSRL). 
GOP 
initially 
prepared and 
submitted 
the 
proposal 
for 
this 
project 
to 
the Bank. 
During 
the 
Project 
preparation period, 
the 
GOP 
instituted 
the 
National 
Public 
Investment System 
(Sistemu 
Nucionul 
de 
Znversio'n 
Pu'blicu, 
SNIP) 
which 
requires 
that 
all 
investment projects, 
regardless 
of 
the 
source 
of 
funding, 
be subjected 
to 
rigorous technical evaluation. 
Although 
compliance 
with 
the requirements 
of 
the 
SNP 
was 
a 
cause 
for 
significant delay 
in 
Project preparation, 
it 
contributed 
to 
strengthening Project ownership 
on 
the 
part 
of 
MED 
and 
MOF. 
5. 
Value 
added 
of 
Bank 
support 
in 
this 
project: 
The 
World 
Bank 
has 
extensive 
experience 
in 
supporting 
projects 
on 
rural 
education, 
with 
strong 
multigrade, 
bilingual 
and 
intercultural 
components. 
Also, 
the 
Bank 
has 
wide 
experience 
in 
-28- 
Page  35
decentralization 
programs 
where 
local 
communities 
play 
an 
important 
role. 
The 
experience 
gained 
in 
Central 
America, 
Brazil 
and 
Colombia 
in 
these 
areas 
will 
be 
instrumental 
in 
assisting 
MED's 
efforts 
in 
developing 
quality 
education 
in 
rural 
areas. 
The 
Bank 
has 
already 
been 
working 
with 
GOP extensively 
in 
the 
early 
implementation 
of 
the 
proposed 
reforms. 
The 
project 
is 
tied 
to 
the Programmatic 
Social 
Reform 
Loans  (PSRL 
I 
and 
II) 
which 
is 
promoting 
the 
development 
of 
the 
new 
teacher 
career 
structure 
and 
the 
piloting 
of 
a 
rural 
teacher 
incentive 
program, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
first 
steps 
toward 
decentralization and community participation. 
E. 
Summary Project 
Analysis 
(Detailed 
assessments 
are 
in 
the 
project file, 
see 
Annex 
8) 
1. 
Economic 
(see 
Annex 
4): 
Cost 
benefit 
0 
Cost effectiveness 
0 
Other (specify) 
NPV=US$ 
million; 
ERR 
= 
15 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
The 
results 
of 
the 
cost 
benefit 
analysis 
are 
based 
on 
relatively 
conservative 
and 
alternative 
scenarios 
that 
were 
constructed 
using 
various 
assumptions 
of 
improvements 
in 
efficiency 
rates, 
including 
productivity, 
repetition 
and 
dropout 
rates 
resulting 
from 
the  implementation 
of 
the 
project.  Each 
scenario 
was 
compared 
to 
a 
baseline 
(without 
project) 
that 
assumes 
a 
decrease 
of 
10 
percent on 
repetition 
and 
dropout 
rates 
on 
primary 
and 
secondary 
levels. 
The 
analysis 
shows 
that the 
project 
is 
viable 
from 
an 
economic 
point 
of 
view. 
Despite 
being 
conservative 
in 
terms 
of 
the expected 
improvement 
in 
external 
efficiency 
rates and 
with 
conservative 
assumptions 
of 
GDP 
growth, 
ERR 
(Economic 
Rate 
of 
Return) 
estimates 
fall 
in 
the 
range 
of 
8 
to 
16 
percent 
for 
the 
initial 
education intervention. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
primary 
education, 
the 
estimated 
ERR 
fall 
in 
the 
range 
of 
8 
to 
23 
percent. 
In 
a reasonable 
scenario, 
in 
which 
primary completion 
rates reach 
90 
percent 
in 
10 
years, 
the 
ERR 
ranges 
between 
1 
1.4 
percent 
and 
17.7 
percent, 
depending 
on 
assumptions 
regarding 
once 
and 
for 
all 
small 
increases 
in 
productivity 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
improvement 
of 
quality 
of 
education. 
The 
results 
of 
the 
analysis 
should 
be 
considered 
as 
a 
lower 
bound, 
given 
that the 
economic 
indicators 
used 
were 
quite 
conservative 
and 
the 
estimates 
of 
the 
ERR 
do 
not 
take 
into 
account 
all 
externalities 
resulting 
from 
the 
Project. 
In 
addition, 
several 
components 
of 
the project, 
such 
as 
the 
teacher 
training 
institutions 
accreditation 
system, 
the 
institutional 
strengthening and the 
reform 
of 
the 
teachers' career are 
elements that 
will 
benefit 
all 
Peruvian 
students. 
Thus, 
the analysis 
leads 
to 
the conclusion 
that 
there 
is 
reasonable 
certainty that 
a comprehensive 
intervention 
in 
rural 
education, 
such 
as 
this 
Project, should 
effect 
changes 
in 
key 
education indicators, 
as 
long 
as 
it 
is 
implemented appropriately 
and 
as 
planned. 
2. 
Financial 
(see 
Annex 
4 
and 
Annex 
5): 
NPV=US$ 
million; 
FRR 
= 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
Several 
alternative 
scenarios 
of 
expenditures 
for 
the 
following 
years, 
and 
the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
costs 
over 
total 
expenditures 
were 
calculated. 
There 
are 
only preliminary 
figures 
for 
overall 
educational 
expenditures 
in 
2003. 
Between 
2000 and 
2001 there 
was 
a 
reduction 
in 
educational 
expenditures. 
During 
the 
first 
half 
of 
2001, 
with 
the 
Transition 
Government, 
there 
was 
a 
contraction 
of 
fiscal 
expenditures. 
Later, 
in 
the 
second 
half 
of 
the 
year, 
the 
new government increased 
expenditures 
slightly, 
including 
a 
9 
percent 
raise 
in 
teachers salaries, 
which 
was 
the 
first 
increase 
in 
pensionable 
salaries 
in 
25 
years. 
In 
2002, 
public 
educational expenditures 
were 
US$ 
1.71 
billion, 
representing 
3 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
Given 
the 
priorities 
established 
by 
the 
current 
administration, 
it 
is 
likely 
that 
resources 
devoted 
to 
-29- 
Page  36
education 
will 
increase. 
The 
GOP 
has 
established, 
although 
not formally, 
two 
specific 
targets, one 
is 
the 
increase 
of 
the 
share 
of 
education 
in 
the 
public 
budget 
from 
the current 
19 
percent 
to 
30 
percent 
in 
2006. 
The 
second 
one 
is 
doubling 
teachers' salaries 
by 
that 
year. 
It 
will 
probably be 
difficult 
to 
meet 
any 
of 
these 
targets. 
There 
are 
two 
main 
points 
that 
can 
be 
derived 
from 
the 
financial 
analysis. 
First, 
the current 
size 
of 
the 
public 
sector 
is 
small 
to 
sustain 
an 
important 
increase 
in 
educational 
expenditures. 
A 
reform 
will 
be 
required 
to 
restructure 
the 
public 
sector 
in 
order 
to 
increase 
the 
share 
of 
educational 
expenditures. 
Second, 
any 
attainable 
change 
in 
the structure 
of 
expenditures 
within 
the 
public 
sector 
will 
fall 
short 
of 
what 
is 
needed 
to 
increase expenditures 
in 
education. 
The 
country requires 
an increase 
in 
the 
overall 
ratio 
of 
government expenditures 
as 
a 
percentage 
of 
GDP. 
However, 
for 
any 
increase 
in 
this 
line 
to 
be 
effective, 
a 
previous 
reform 
of 
the 
public 
sector 
is 
required. 
Fiscal 
Impact: 
The 
fiscal 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
is 
relatively 
small. Calculations were 
made 
taking 
into 
accountted the 
state 
counterpart, the 
implicit 
additional fiscal 
costs 
due 
to 
higher 
enrollments 
generated 
by 
both 
the 
initial 
education 
and 
basic 
education interventions, recurrent 
costs and debt 
service. 
The 
financial 
impact 
has 
been 
calculated 
using 
the 
scenario 
of 
the 
economic 
analysis 
which 
assumes 
a 
reduction 
in 
repetition 
rates 
of 
50 
percent 
and 
dropout 
rates 
of 
75 
percent 
by 
2009. 
Until 
2009, 
all 
implicit, 
recurrent 
and 
direct project 
expenditures amount 
to 
less 
than 
1 
percent 
of 
the 
overall 
expenditures 
in 
education, 
reaching 
at 
most 
0.78 
percent 
of 
additional 
costs. 
Also, 
they 
represent 
less 
than 
2 percent 
of 
current expenditures 
in 
initial 
and basic 
education. Recurrent 
costs are 
mainly 
training 
expenditures and materials. 
They 
increase 
sharply 
in 
the 
two 
years 
following 
phase 
one due 
to 
training 
interventions that 
start 
in 
2004 and 
2005 
that last 
for 
three 
years. 
Afterwards, 
these 
costs 
fall. 
However, 
as 
a 
proportion 
of 
rural 
expenditures, 
the 
project 
has 
a 
larger 
impact. 
As 
shown, 
it 
amounts 
to 
4.3 
percent 
of 
overall 
projected 
expenditures. 
This 
figure might 
be under 
estimated, 
because 
it 
is 
assumed 
that 
expenditures 
per 
student 
do 
not 
vary 
between 
rural 
and urban 
areas. 
Currently, 
rural 
expenditures 
per 
pupil 
are 
probably 
lower 
than 
in 
urban 
areas, 
so 
the 
increase 
in 
expenditures  related 
to 
this 
project 
may 
even be 
more significant 
in 
relation 
to 
what 
is 
currently 
spent 
in 
these 
areas. 
In 
addition, expenditures 
in 
rural 
areas 
may 
increase sharply 
two 
or 
three 
years 
from 
now 
if 
as 
part 
of 
the 
reform 
in 
teachers' career 
there 
is 
an increase 
in 
salaries 
and 
bonuses 
in 
order 
to 
attract 
high 
performance 
teachers 
to rural 
areas.

Technical: 
The 
following 
issues 
will 
be

in 
order 
to

continued  focus  on 
rural 
children: 
(i)

(ii)

(iii)

that defines roles 
and 
responsibilities 
at 
all 
levels; (iv) 
the 
links 
with 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
Ministry 
of Women 
and 
Social 
Development 
for

ECD

to

and 
nutrition 
education 
are 
adequately 
included 
in 
the programs 
and; 
(v) 
follow-up on 
overall

with CIAS 
(Comision

de

-

will

The

have 
in

SIAF

i.e.

In

the

of

(including

The

of 
the 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
insignificant 
since 
only  rehabilitation 
of 
schools 
will

MED 
is 
not projecting 
any

in 
primary

any 
infrastructure 
for 
the 
preschool 
classrooms 
or

would

to 
existing

will

6. 
Social: 
6.1 
Summarize 
key social 
issues 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
objectives, and specify 
the 
project's social 
development 
outcomes. 
A 
review 
of 
the 
legal 
framework 
shows 
that ethnic, 
cultural 
and 
language 
diversity 
are 
recognized, 
valued 
and 
supported 
by 
the 
State. 
The constitution 
upholds 
universal 
human 
rights, 
recognizes 
ethnic 
and 
cultural 
diversity 
and 
all 
native 
languages 
as 
official 
in 
Peru, 
wherever 
spoken. 
The 
General 
Law 
of 
Education 
as 
well 
as 
other laws related 
to 
education 
(including 
the 
LO 
Convention 
No. 
169) 
decree 
that 
bilingual 
education 
is 
a 
right for 
all 
indigenous 
peoples whenever feasible. 
These 
principles 
are 
all 
reflected 
in 
the 
Supreme 
Decree 
creating 
the 
"Direcci6n 
de 
Educaci6n 
Bilingue 
Intercultural" 
currently 
responsible 
for 
the 
bilingual 
education program. 
A 
beneficiary 
assessment 
was 
carried 
out 
during 
the 
preparation 
of 
the  project. 
The 
Bilingual 
Intercultural 
Education Program 
has 
been evaluated 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
educational and social 
impacts. 
In 
addition, there 
have 
been numerous 
consultation 
processes 
(see 
6.2) 
which 
have 
raised 
key 
issues 
that 
- 
32 
- 
Page  39
have 
been 
included 
into 
the 
project 
design. 
There 
are 
four 
main 
groups 
of 
issues 
identified which 
are 
being 
incorporated 
into 
the 
project 
design 
and 
are 
reflected 
in 
the 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
Strategy 
(see 
Annex 
11): 
Pedagogical practices 
in 
the classroom. 
Even though 
the 
curricular 
reform 
has 
had 
an 
impact 
in 
the 
classroom 
in 
terms 
of 
promoting 
a 
more 
horizontal 
and 
group-work  oriented 
learning, 
there 
is 
still 
evidence 
that 
teachers 
remain hierarchical 
and 
may 
show a 
discriminatory 
behavior, 
particularly 
to 
indigenous children. 
Bilingual 
materials 
are 
welcome 
by 
teachers, 
but 
further  training 
is 
needed 
to 
ensure 
full 
utilization 
in 
the 
classroom. 
The 
use 
of 
the mother tongue 
is 
common 
but 
is 
typically mixed 
with 
Spanish 
in 
a haphazard way. 
The 
reason 
for 
this 
may 
be 
the 
lack 
of 
materials 
for 
teaching 
Spanish 
as 
a 
second 
language 
and 
that 
parents 
press 
teachers 
to 
introduce 
Spanish 
as 
early 
as 
possible. 
There 
are 
indications that 
vernacular 
languages 
are 
being 
revalued 
socially 
thanks 
to 
their 
use 
in 
the classroom. 
The 
curricular 
reform 
and 
the 
limited 
introduction 
of 
materials and 
teacher 
training 
are 
still 
not 
reflected 
in 
student 
performance results 
where 
third 
grade 
children 
still 
have 
difficulties 
in 
reading 
and 
writing 
in 
their 
mother 
tongue. 
Children 
often 
need 
to 
share 
their schooling 
with 
chores 
in 
the 
house 
or 
farm, 
with 
the 
consequent 
absences, 
particularly during 
harvest periods. 
Community 
participation. 
Parents' 
participation 
has 
been 
traditionally 
limited 
to 
the 
contribution 
of 
manual labor 
in 
the 
conservation 
and 
rehabilitation 
of 
school infrastructure.  Whenever 
possible, parents 
also 
make 
financial 
contributions 
to 
purchase 
basic 
goods 
for 
the 
school. 
This 
limited 
participation 
results 
mainly 
because 
traditionally 
schools 
have 
kept 
parents 
aside. 
Thus, 
parents 
are 
not 
aware 
about 
the  objectives 
of 
the 
bilingual 
education 
and 
the 
advantages 
in 
the 
use 
of 
mother 
tongue 
in 
their 
children's 
education. 
The project 
will 
address these 
issues 
by 
creating 
flexibility 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
curriculum, 
promoting 
active 
and 
effective 
community participation. 
Training 
of 
teachers 
in 
multigrade classroom 
management 
skills, 
cultural 
awareness, 
planning 
and 
bilingual 
education (where pertinent) 
will 
also be 
key 
areas, 
linked 
to 
teacher 
incentives. 
Greater 
supervision and support 
to 
teachers 
will 
also 
receive 
prominent attention 
in 
the 
Project.  School 
autonomy actions 
will 
include 
community 
participation 
both 
as 
contributors 
to 
the 
educational 
process 
as 
well 
as 
a 
means 
for 
accountability.

education

National

During

33 
-

challenge 
of

(“La  Educacidn

Ombudsman’s

and 
from 
the

(PROMUDEH).

process

on the

for

of

as 
diverse 
a

as

en

(National  Policies 
on

6.3 
How 
does 
the 
project 
involve 
consultations 
or collaboration 
with 
NGOs 
or 
other 
civil 
society 
organizations? 
NGOs 
have 
had 
a 
key 
role 
in 
the development 
of 
the 
national 
rural 
education 
policy 
and 
of 
the 
bilingual 
intercultural 
education 
policy. 
Both 
policy 
documents  were 
developed  through 
workshops 
and 
consultation 
meetings 
with 
wide participation 
of 
NGOs 
involved 
in 
rural 
and 
bilingual 
education. 
In 
addition, 
NGOs 
have 
had 
a 
prominent 
role 
in 
the consultation 
processes 
described 
in 
6.2 
(see 
Annex 
11 
for 
the 
list 
of 
the main 
NGOs 
and 
civil 
society 
organizations). 
A 
Consultative Committee 
was 
established 
in 
May 
2001 
to 
promote 
the 
participation 
of civil 
society 
and 
beneficiaries 
of 
bilingual 
education. 
Members 
include 
representatives 
of 
the 
major national 
indigenous 
peoples 
organizations 
as 
well 
as 
academic 
institutions.  The Committee 
has 
met three 
times 
reviewing 
the 
bilingual 
strategic 
plan 
and 
the organization 
of 
the 
DINEBI. 
In 
addition 
DINEBI 
is 
represented 
in 
the 
“Comisidn 
Nacional 
de  Pueblos 
Andinos,  Amazdnicos 
y 
Afro 
Peruanos” 
(National 
Committee  on 
Andean and 
Amazon 
People’s) 
which 
is 
lead 
by 
the 
First 
Lady 
of 
Peru. 
During 
the preparation 
process, 
the 
Bank 
has 
had 
extensive contact 
with 
NGOs 
linked 
to 
rural 
education. 
NGOs 
have 
provided 
significant contributions 
to 
the 
design 
process 
in 
terms 
of 
what 
is 
feasible 
and 
the 
key 
areas 
that 
need 
to 
be 
addressed 
to improve quality 
of 
education 
in 
rural 
areas. 
It 
is 
expected that 
NGOs 
will 
contribute 
to 
project  implementation 
by 
providing 
teacher 
training 
and support 
to 
local 
networks. 
6.4 
What institutional 
arrangements have 
been 
provided 
to 
ensure 
the 
project 
achieves 
its 
social 
development outcomes? 
In 
order 
to 
address 
the 
main 
issues 
found 
with 
respect 
to bilingual 
and 
intercultural 
education, 
MED 
has 
developed 
a 
10 
step 
strategy 
which 
revolves  around strengthening 
on 
one 
hand, 
the 
links 
with 
the 
community, 
and, 
on 
the 
other, the 
pedagogical 
processes 
in 
the 
classroom: 
Community 
Consultation 
and 
Participation 
Program. 
MED 
has 
planned 
a 
continuous  consultation 
process 
on 
the 
design, 
execution 
and 
evaluation 
of 
the proposed 
strategies 
and 
activities. 
This 
process 
will 
complement 
other 
consultation 
processes 
already 
included 
in 
the 
project 
design 
such 
as 
the School 
Councils 
and 
Network 
Councils. 
The 
main 
instruments 
for 
the consultation 
process 
will 
include 
“Mesas 
de 
DiBlogo”, 
Regional  Consultative  Councils,  indigenous 
and 
bilingual 
teachers 
conferences, 
and 
intercultural 
events 
directed 
to 
students, 
teachers 
and 
parents. 
A 
strong dissemination 
program 
is 
also 
planned 
in 
order 
to 
raise 
awareness 
to 
the parents 
on the value 
of 
the 
bilingual 
program, 
the 
need 
for 
and 
value 
of 
intercultural 
education 
and 
promotion 
of 
self-esteem. 
Strengthening Pedagogical 
Processes.

has 
already produced materials 
in 
15 
Andean  and 
Amazon basin 
languages 
for 
math,  language, 
social  and natural 
sciences, 
and 
Spanish 
as 
a

The 
strategy 
calls 
for 
further 
validation 
and 
development 
of 
new complementary materials 
for 
-34- 
Page  41
students 
and self-paced

Secondly, the strategy aims 
at

and

teacher 
training 
on 
bilingual 
and

on

peoples 
on

children’s

Finally,

to

Annex 
2: 
Detailed Project Description 
PERU: 
Rural Education Project 
The 
Project-Phase 
I 
of 
a 
three 
phase 
program- 
aims 
to 
support 
the 
Peruvian government 
in 
improving 
basic 
education 
quality 
and 
reducing education disparities between 
rural 
and 
urban 
areas. 
The objective 
will 
be 
achieved 
by: 
(a) 
increasing 
access 
to 
and 
completion 
rates 
in 
rural 
schools; 
(b) 
improving 
teachers 
development 
and 
performance, and 
upgrading 
schoolklassroom learning 
environment, 
and 
teachers’ 
performance;  and 
(c) 
fostering  closer-to-school 
and 
learning 
oriented 
management  strategies. 
The 
expected 
end 
result 
would 
be 
more 
and 
better 
learning 
outcomes 
for 
an 
increased 
number 
of 
rural 
school 
children. 
Project implementation 
strategy 
will 
be 
two-pronged, to: 
(a) 
strengthen and 
expand 
selected 
activities 
where 
prior 
albeit exploratory implementation 
has 
proven 
successful, 
as 
for 
example 
bilingual 
education 
materials 
and 
teacher 
training; 
and 
(b) 
include innovative 
elements that have 
had 
limited 
implementation 
and 
whose 
success has 
not 
been 
fully 
demonstrated, such 
as 
the distance 
secondary 
education program. 
Implementation 
of 
these 
actions 
will 
be 
carried  out 
in 
11 
primarily 
rural 
departments 
(Amazonas, 
Ancash, 
Ayacucho, 
Cajamarca, Cusco, 
Huancavelica, 
Loreto, 
Piura, 
Puno, 
San 
Martin, 
and 
Ucayali) 
and 
within 
at 
least 
290 
rural 
school networks. 
By 
Component: 
Project 
Component 
1 
- 
US$10.54 
million 
Expanding Education 
Access 
for 
Rural 
Children 
(1 
1.2 
percent 
of 
total 
Project 
cost) 
Objective. 
The key objective 
of 
this 
component 
is 
to 
increase 
access 
to 
quality initial, 
preschool 
and 
secondary 
education 
for 
rural 
children 
who 
have 
been 
excluded 
from 
these  services. 
In 
spite 
of 
pioneering 
ECD 
initiatives 
in 
the 
1960s, 
Peru 
shows 
today 
a 
surprisingly 
low 
coverage 
rate 
for 
the 
0-2 
year-old group (around 
3 
percent nationwide, 
and 
1 
percent 
in 
rural 
areas), 
and the 
3-5 
year-old group (42 
percent 
nationally, 
and 
36 
percent 
in 
rural 
areas). 
Project 
strategies 
include 
the 
following 
demand-based 
initiatives: 
(a) 
initial 
and 
preschool education programs 
designed 
to 
promote 
a 
balanced early 
childhood 
development  (ECD); 
and 
(b) 
distance 
secondary 
education  programs 
through 
the 
application 
of 
cost-effective 
information 
and communication 
technologies. 
1.1 
Access 
to 
Quality 
Initial 
Education 
and 
Preschool 
Education 
(US$2.71 
million) 
Objective. 
This 
sub-component 
is 
designed 
to 
expand 
access 
of 
young 
rural 
children 
(0-5 
years 
old) 
to 
a 
variety 
of 
existing or 
brand-new 
ECD 
programs. 
These 
programs 
aim 
at 
both 
meeting 
young children’s 
educational 
and 
psychological 
needs 
and 
making them 
ready 
for 
primary 
and 
further 
school 
achievement. 
Implementation 
arrangements. 
Two 
distinct 
groups 
are addressed 
under: 
(a) 
the 
0 
through 
2-years 
old 
children participating 
in 
ECD 
programs; and 
(b) 
the 
3-5 
year-old group 
being 
prepared 
(at 
preschool) 
for 
the 
adequate 
entry 
to formal 
primary 
schooling. 
MED 
relative 
success 
with 
the 
latter 
group 
is 
largely 
due 
to 
the 
development 
of 
the 
Non-formal 
Initial 
Education 
Program (PRONOEI) 
and 
the 
Initial 
Education 
Centers (CEO. 
- 
45 
- 
Page  52
, 
For 
children 
0-2 
years  old, 
a 
non-formal 
approach 
will 
be 
implemented 
in 
which 
children 
will 
be 
educated 
along 
with 
their 
parents 
and 
siblings 
within 
a 
family 
and 
community 
framework. 
Services 
will 
be 
delivered 
by 
trained community 
educators 
(animadoras). 
Wherever relevant, community 
educators 
fluent 
in 
the 
local 
vemacular 
languages 
will 
be 
trained 
for 
the 
provision 
of 
Initial 
Education. 
During 
Phase 
I, 
three models 
of 
Initial 
Education 
will 
be validated, 
depending 
on 
the

and

of

as

The

will 
function 
with 
one 
animudora, 
who 
will 
visit 
each

a

covering

10 
children. 
The

will 
offer

and

through

benefiting 
approximately 
10 
children 
daily 
at 
a

designated 
for 
this

by

The

named 
family 
education, 
will

out 
by 
one 
animadora 
in 
charge 
of 
close 
to 
30 families 
(groups

each 
and 
will 
focus on the improvement 
of 
child-rearing 
practices. 
Active family 
participation 
is 
a 
key 
factor 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
these 
strategies. 
Through  the 
above 
mentioned 
programs, 
this 
sub-component 
seeks 
to 
benefit 
approximately 
30,000 
children 
with 
2,000 
animadoras. 
The program would, 
then, 
increase coverage 
from 
3.9 
percent 
to 
5.7 
percent 
at 
the 
national 
level.  The 
following 
will

during

of

(a)

(b)

of 
rural 
families'

(c) 
detailed

of 
the 
three

(d) 
preparation 
of 
manuals 
for 
animadoras, 
teaching 
coordinators, and 
parents; 
(e) 
printing 
and

of 
manuals; 
(f) 
production 
and

of 
pertaining 
cassettes 
and videos; 
(8) 
selection 
and

of 
educational materials 
for 
children; 
and, 
(h)

and

of 
training

for 
animudoras 
and

During

will

Training

will

the

During

This

For

3-5

1.2 
Secondary Distance 
Education 
(US$7.83 
million) 
Objective. 
This 
sub-component 
is 
designed 
to 
provide 
technical 
support 
to 
MEDs 
effort 
seeking 
alternative 
models 
for 
access 
to 
secondary 
education 
in 
selected 
rural 
areas 
through 
the 
application 
of 
cost-effective 
information 
and 
communication technologies. 
A 
Phase 
I 
enrollment 
of 
5,000 
students, 
a 
14 
percent increase 
in 
coverage 
as 
compared 
to 
the 2002 
rates 
in 
the target 
areas, 
is 
expected. 
Secondary 
education 
is 
compulsory 
in 
Peru 
and 
has 
become 
part 
of 
the 
basic 
education 
structure. 
As 
a 
result 
of 
increased 
six-year 
primary 
education 
availability, 
effectiveness 
and 
efficiency 
in 
recent 
years, 
more 
and more Peruvian 
students 
are 
looking for 
secondary 
education. 
In 
rural 
areas, 
however, 
access 
to 
post-primary 
schooling,  even 
the 
five-year 
basic 
secondary 
education, 
is 
made 
particularly 
difficult, 
especially 
for 
those 
groups 
who 
have 
to 
face 
either 
geographic 
constraints 
in 
the 
Andean mountains 
or 
sheer 
remoteness 
in 
the 
Amazon forest. 
Any 
impact-oriented 
solution 
must 
identify 
creative 
strategies 
other 
than 
traditional 
school delivery. 
Zmplementation 
arrangements. 
Phase 
I 
will 
be 
a 
piloting, 
experimenting 
and 
comparative 
assessment 
segment. 
The focus 
will 
be on testing 
and 
gauging 
several 
secondary 
education models 
in 
order 
to 
determine 
the 
cost-effective  solutions that  better 
fit 
each 
particular 
rural 
environment 
of 
Peru. 
The 
curriculum 
contents 
of 
the 
three models 
(including 
the 
presence 
one) 
must 
be the 
same. 
Two 
models 
will 
be 
under consideration: 
(a) 
Model 
A 
- 
The 
strategy adopted 
by 
the 
Pilot 
Distance 
Education 
Centers 
(CPED) 
uses 
custom-made 
or 
adapted  videotapes, 
printed 
materials 
and 
Receive 
Only 
(RO) 
video 
transmission. 
A 
tutor 
works 
as 
a 
facilitator, 
and 
itinerant 
teacher 
visits 
the 
schools 
periodically, 
assesses 
-47- 
Page  54
progress 
and 
provides pedagogical 
support 
to 
tutors. 
MED 
is 
currently implementing 
this 
model 
in 
101 
CPEDs 
throughout 
Peru. 
For 
the 
evaluation, 
a representative 
sample 
will 
be 
selected 
for 
comparison 
purposes; 
and 
(b) 
Model 
B, 
proposed 
by 
MED 
and 
currently 
under 
design, 
is 
a 
more complex instrument 
involving: 
(i) 
Model 
A; 
(ii) 
satellite-linked 
VSAT 
(Very 
Small 
Aperture 
Terminal) 
bidirectional 
channels 
to 
facilitate 
real-time communication between the 
students 
and a 
tutor 
located 
at 
the 
emitting 
station, 
printed 
handbooks, videos, 
computer, 
CD-ROM, 
and 
Internet 
access. 
These 
two 
models 
will 
be 
compared 
with 
each 
other 
and 
with 
secondary  presence 
schools 
according 
to 
indicators 
showing 
students’ 
attitudinal 
and 
behavioral 
features 
associated 
with 
the 
interaction 
with 
each 
specific 
delivery 
model, 
including: 
(i) 
satisfaction 
with 
the program, 
knowledge 
and other 
skills 
acquired, 
frustration 
faced; 
(ii) 
satisfaction 
with 
the 
school 
environment, educational 
resources 
and 
technologies 
used; 
and 
(iii) 
student 
performance 
and achievement. 
A 
detailed 
and 
participatory 
analysis 
of 
this 
comparative 
assessment 
will 
help 
determine 
the 
model 
or 
models 
for 
the 
expansion 
of 
the 
access 
to 
basic 
secondary 
education 
in 
rural 
Peru 
during 
Phases 
II 
and 
III. 
Prior  to 
implementation,  the  models 
will 
be  validated  through 
a 
consultative 
process, 
and 
the 
detailed implementation 
plan 
will 
be 
reviewed 
jointly 
with 
the 
World 
Bank. 
The 
pilot 
will 
be 
implemented gradually 
during 
the three-year 
period 
up 
to 
a 
total 
of 
116 
schools 
which 
will 
be located 
at 
the 
center 
of 
the 
rural 
networks 
which 
do not 
have 
secondary 
schools. 
It 
is 
expected 
that 
facilities 
will 
also 
be 
used 
for 
teacher 
training 
and 
possibly enrichment 
of 
upper

The program 
will 
adopt 
the 
same 
basic 
curriculum 
officially 
established 
for 
the  regular 
secondary 
education, 
aiming 
at 
promoting 
meaningful,

students’,

processes 
and 
outcomes, 
with

linguistic

the 
beneficiary 
community, specifically,  concerning 
treatment 
of 
Spanish 
as 
a 
second 
language. Secondary 
education 
competences 
and 
curriculum 
are 
currently being reviewed 
under 
an 
IDB 
financed project. 
Financing. 
Investments 
will 
made 
to: 
(a) 
acquire new 
information

of

Continuous Teacher 
Development 
System 
(CTDS) 
(US$24.86

This

upgrade

thereby

the dichotomous and

the 
initial,

and

required

adequate

Project

48

Implementation

75 
percent

120

(25)  and

updated

15

US$5.000 
unit

The

“Amauta”

by

ISP 
or 
share

2.2 
Curriculum 
Adjustment Provision 
of 
Educational 
Materials 
(US$9.09 
million) 
Objective. 
This 
sub-component 
seeks 
to 
contribute 
to 
quality 
improvement 
of 
rural 
primary 
schooling, 
including 
areas 
in 
which 
one 
or 
more  indigenous 
languages  are 
predominantly 
spoken. 
Two 
complementary 
models 
will 
be 
implemented: 
(a) 
delivery 
at 
national 
level 
of 
already experimented 
bilingual 
educational materials prepared 
by 
the 
MED, 
(b) 
the 
design and 
validation 
within 
the 
networks 
of 
a new educational proposal 
for 
the 
rural 
areas, 
aiming 
at 
more 
pertinent 
pedagogical 
service, 
through 
- 
49 
- 
Page  56
high 
quality 
self-paced 
learning materials and 
teacher manuals 
to 
support 
multigrade 
instruction 
and 
bilingual 
education. 
In 
both 
cases, 
rural 
indigenous vemacular-speaking 
children 
and 
Spanish-speaking 
children 
will 
be 
benefited 
by 
the 
project. 
Teacher 
training 
activities 
will 
also 
be 
included 
in 
both 
modalities. 
Although 
90 
percent 
of 
the 
schools 
in 
rural 
areas 
in 
Peru 
are 
multigrade, 
they 
have 
never 
been 
supplied 
with 
appropriately 
designed 
materials 
to 
support 
multigrade 
teaching 
or 
bilingual 
instruction. 
Colombia’s 
Escuela 
Nueva 
and 
similar 
programs 
in 
other countries 
have demonstrated 
that supplying 
children 
in 
rural 
areas 
with 
self-paced 
learning materials and other classroom materials 
(rincones 
de 
lectura, 
for 
instance) 
that 
permit 
teachers 
to 
engage 
in 
effective  multigrade instruction 
results 
in 
higher 
student 
leaming 
and 
lower repetition 
rates. 
Self-paced 
learning 
materials 
have also been associated 
with 
lower 
dropout 
among 
rural 
students, because 
they 
allow rural 
children 
to 
make 
continued 
academic 
progress 
despite 
discontinuous 
school 
attendance, 
due 
to 
families’ 
needs, 
farm 
labor, 
illnesses, 
or 
other 
disruptions. 
Similarly, 
research 
on 
indigenous communities 
in 
Guatemala, 
Bolivia 
and 
elsewhere 
has 
shown 
that supporting indigenous 
children 
with 
bilingual 
instructional 
materials 
in 
the 
first 
schooling 
years 
helps 
lower 
repetition 
and 
dropout 
rates 
and boosts 
learning 
in 
all 
subjects. 
Zmplementation 
arrangements. 
For 
rural 
Spanish-speaking 
students, 
the  Project  includes 
the 
validation 
and implementation 
within 
the 
networks 
of 
a 
self-paced 
rural 
educational 
model 
which allows 
students 
to 
progress 
according 
to 
their 
own individual rhythm 
and 
leaming

and

to

2 10

In

In

In

Project Component 
3 
- 
US$20.08

Reform 
of 
Teacher 
Policy

Education Management 
(21.3

total project

Objective. 
A 
key 
objective 
of 
this 
component 
is 
to 
support 
the 
new 
Government’s 
efforts 
to 
promote 
management-related

by 
addressing 
politically 
sensitive 
issues 
in 
areas 
that 
have been 
among 
the 
most 
important 
constraints 
to 
educational 
progress 
in 
Peru. 
Although 
the 
reforms 
of 
teacher 
policy 
and 
education 
management 
will 
be 
national 
in 
scope, 
they  have considerable 
implications 
for  rural 
schools, 
which 
have 
suffered 
the 
most 
from 
teacher 
low 
quality,  and 
motivation 
linked 
to 
inadequate 
standards 
and incentives, 
and 
a centralized 
system 
of 
education 
management 
that 
has 
failed to 
monitor 
and 
support 
rural 
schools. 
To 
meet 
these 
issues, 
this 
component 
has 
six 
inter-related 
sub-components: 
(a) 
teacher 
policy 
reform; 
(b) 
reform 
of 
education 
management; 
(c) 
financial 
support 
for 
school 
development; 
(d) 
national 
assessment 
system; (e) 
policy 
research; 
and 
(f) 
Project 
management. 
3.1 
Teacher 
Policy 
Reform 
(US$l.60 
million) 
Objective. 
The 
purpose 
of 
this 
sub-component 
is 
to 
support 
MED 
efforts 
to 
boost teaching 
quality 
by 
developing and 
implementing 
a 
standard-based teacher  career 
and development 
reform 
relying 
on 
a 
professional performance evaluation 
mechanism, 
a 
certification 
system, 
and 
a 
set 
of 
instruments 
for 
remuneration 
based 
on 
recognition. 
The 
diagnosis 
of 
the sector 
shows 
a 
lack 
of 
clarity 
as 
to 
what 
students are 
expected 
to 
learn, 
as 
well 
as 
to 
the 
instructional 
practices 
most 
likely to 
work 
in 
the 
highly 
differentiated 
cultural 
and 
socioeconomic 
contexts 
of 
Peru. 
This, 
combined 
with 
fragmented 
and 
unskilled 
management 
controls, 
and scarce 
or 
inappropriate incentives, explains 
the persistence 
of 
low 
education 
quality 
and 
the 
difficulty 
of 
breaking 
- 
52 
- 
Page  59
the 
vicious cycle. 
Clarifying 
the 
expectations 
would provide 
the 
system 
with 
a 
sense 
of 
direction, 
and 
aligning 
all 
the 
elements 
of 
the 
teacher 
training 
strategy 
onto 
agreed-upon 
standards 
would 
promote 
internal 
coherence between 
policy 
statements 
and 
classroom 
practices. 
Implementation 
arrangements. 
The Project 
would 
include: 
(a) 
the 
design 
and 
initial 
implementation 
of 
the 
teacher  career and 
development 
system, 
including: 
(i) 
a 
detailed structure 
of 
the 
teaching 
career 
ladder, 
its 
performance 
and 
experience 
levels 
and 
corresponding 
promotion 
criteria; 
(ii) 
a systematic 
teaching performance evaluation covering 
the 
multidimensional intra- 
and 
extra-classroom roles 
played 
by 
teachers 
in 
their  daily 
educational activities; 
(iii) 
a teacher’s 
initial 
certification 
of 
professional 
quality, 
and 
periodic re-certification 
system. 
The 
re-certification 
process 
is 
expected 
to 
be, 
initially, 
on 
a 
voluntary 
basis and 
later shifted 
to 
compulsory 
status; 
(iv) 
the established 
scenarios 
for 
the 
remuneration 
package and  other 
forms 
of 
incentives 
and 
recognition; 
(b) 
the 
development and 
dissemination 
of 
guidelines, operating 
manuals, 
software 
and 
other instruments 
to 
share 
with 
all 
teachers 
and 
managers 
information 
on 
the 
benefits 
legally 
guaranteed 
and 
conditions 
set 
by 
the 
teacher 
development 
system: (c) 
the 
upgrading 
of 
the 
teacher 
MIS 
(Management 
Information 
System) 
to 
support 
information-based 
teacher management 
and 
deployment. 
The 
h4IS 
will 
maintain 
an 
updated 
data 
bank 
of 
the professional 
development 
status 
of 
every 
single 
teacher, 
starting 
with 
those 
involved 
with 
the 
290 proposed school 
networks. 
This 
information 
system 
will 
become a sine 
qua 
non 
condition 
for 
the 
adequate 
operation 
of 
the 
Continuous 
Teacher 
Development 
System: 
and 
(d) 
the implementation 
of 
several 
national 
and 
local 
workshops 
to 
inform 
teachers  and 
other 
members 
of 
the  education community 
of 
the 
forthcoming 
changes. 
A 
legal 
framework 
for 
the proposed 
teacher 
development 
reform 
hinges 
on the 
existing legislation, 
particularly 
the 
Teachers’ 
Law 
(Ley 
del 
Profesorado) 
and the 
forthcoming 
General 
Education 
Law 
(Ley 
de 
la 
Educacidn 
National) 
and 
the 
Higher 
Education 
Law 
(Ley 
de 
Educacio’n 
Superior), 
which 
will 
rule 
all 
new 
teachers, 
whereas 
the 
current 
ones 
will 
be covered on 
a 
voluntary 
basis. 
By 
2006, 
at 
least 
30 
percent 
of 
all 
teachers 
in 
the 
290 
networks 
are 
expected 
to 
be under 
the new 
legislation. 
By 
the 
end 
of 
Phase 
I, 
the new 
teacher 
professional development structure 
including 
teacher 
performance evaluation 
and salary schedule 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
fully 
in 
place 
in 
the Project 
target 
areas. 
Financing. 
Project 
investments 
would 
include: 
(a) 
consultancies 
with 
a 
variety 
of 
seasoned 
institutional 
experts: 
(b) 
meetings 
to 
discuss 
topics, 
share 
orientation 
and  disseminate 
results 
with 
interested 
stakeholders: 
(c) 
software  acquisition: 
and 
(d) 
pay 
for 
printing 
of 
materials  and  cover 
overall 
sub-component 
implementation. 
3.2 
Reform 
of 
education management 
(US$6.94 
million) 
Objective. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
support 
the  Government’s 
efforts 
to 
“invert 
the 
pyramid” 
of 
education administration 
in 
Peru, 
by 
emphasizing 
the 
focus 
on 
school 
initiatives 
and 
results. 
The 
reform 
will: 
(a) 
increase autonomy 
and 
community voice 
in 
decision-making 
at 
the 
school 
level; 
(b) 
establish 
networks 
in 
rural 
areas 
to 
provide 
support 
for 
isolated 
rural 
schools; 
(c) 
transform the 
existing 
sub-central 
offices 
into 
a 
new 
structure 
for 
local 
supervision 
and 
support 
to 
schools 
at 
a 
provincial 
and 
regional level; 
and 
(d) 
increase 
the accountability 
of 
regional offices 
for 
the performance 
of 
schools 
and other education 
institutions 
under 
their 
jurisdiction. 
Implementation 
arrangements. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
focus 
on: 
(a) 
increasing school autonomy 
by 
(i) 
implanting, 
training 
and 
monitoring 
the 
School 
Council 
in 
3000 
rural 
schools 
as 
a 
mechanism 
for 
community 
expression 
of 
participation 
and 
ownership: 
and 
(ii) 
designing, 
implementing 
and 
disseminating 
the 
Proyectos Educativos Znstitucionales 
- 
PEZ 
in 
at 
least 
200 
rural 
school 
networks 
as 
a 
- 
53 
- 
Page  60
participatory 
planning, implementation, 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
mechanism; 
(b) 
evaluating 
the 
14 
existing 
and 
UDECE-sponsored 
rural 
school 
networks 
so 
that  the experience may be 
improved 
and 
expanded 
into 
the 
290 
networks. 
Lessons 
learned 
would 
be 
incorporated and 
discussed 
within 
a 
planning 
system 
framework 
so 
that 
the 
Project 
would 
be ready 
to 
implement 
future 
rural 
school 
networks, 
train 
their participants 
and 
evaluate 
their activities; 
(c) 
redefining 
and 
assisting 
11 
Regional Offices 
and 
30 
Education 
Management 
Units 
(UGEs) 
and 
their 
respective steering committees 
in 
order 
to 
prepare 
them 
for 
the 
newly 
assigned 
role 
of 
technical  mediators 
between 
schools, 
networks,  “Amauta”  Regional 
Centers 
and 
the 
overall 
system; 
and 
(d) 
fostering  community 
participation 
by: 
(i) 
developing 
and 
disseminating 
the 
concept 
of 
“education 
community” 
involving 
a 
variety 
of 
stakeholders 
(parents, 
teachers, 
NGOs, 
ethnic organizations 
etc.) 
interested 
in 
school mission, role, 
processes 
and 
results; 
(ii) 
challenging 
the 
varied 
support 
deriving 
from 
community-at-large 
into 
School Councils, 
PEIs 
and 
other 
school 
expressions 
and 
projects. 
This 
sub-component 
would 
be 
supported, 
in 
part, 
by 
DFID 
through 
its 
Project 
on 
Rural 
Education. 
This 
Project 
has 
targeted 
its 
activities 
in 
3 
departments 
of 
Peru 
which 
are 
within 
the 
scope 
of 
the 
Project: 
Piura, 
Cusco  and 
San 
Martin. 
The 
Project 
aims 
to 
strengthen 
school  autonomy  through 
the 
implementation 
of 
School 
Councils 
and 
Networks, 
with 
community 
participation. 
The 
instruments 
and 
lessons 
learnt 
from 
these 
interventions 
will 
be 
used 
in 
the strengthening 
of 
school 
autonomy 
in 
the 
remaining Project 
areas. 
Financing. 
Investments 
will 
be 
made 
in: 
(a) 
consulting 
services, 
(b) 
professional and 
parent 
training 
on 
PEI 
design 
and  implementation, 
(c) 
data 
and 
lessons 
dissemination, 
(d) 
stakeholder 
meetings, 
(e) 
equipment 
for 
sub-central 
offices, 
(f) 
process 
in 
outcome 
evaluation,  and 
(g) 
handbooks and 
PEI 
development and 
printing. 
3.3. 
School 
Development 
Fund 
(US$1.76 
million) 
Objective. 
The 
key 
objective 
of 
this 
sub-component 
is 
to 
improve 
local 
school ownership 
and 
governance 
through  the 
support 
of 
School 
and 
Network 
Councils,  design 
and 
implementation 
of 
Institutional 
Educational 
Plans 
(PEIs) 
and 
development 
of 
schools 
networks. PEIs 
are 
expected 
to 
become a 
crucial 
instrument 
for 
the 
development 
of 
local 
participation 
and 
ownership, 
in 
the 
increase 
of 
a 
bottom 
up 
commitment 
process. 
Implementation 
arrangements. 
Since 
the 
ultimate 
objective 
of 
the  education 
system 
is 
to 
support 
students’ 
learning 
in 
development 
improvements 
at 
the school level, 
each 
School 
Council 
will 
develop 
a 
PEI. 
Individual 
schools 
will 
work 
through 
their 
networks 
to 
support 
each 
other 
in 
diagnosing 
problems, 
setting 
priorities, 
and 
developing credible, time-bound action 
plans 
for 
achieving their 
individual 
school 
goals. 
As 
a 
network, 
the  schools 
will 
ensure 
that 
these 
institutional 
plans 
aggregate 
into 
a 
network 
development 
plan 
that 
represents 
a coherent strategy 
for 
the 
micro-region. 
The new 
UGEs 
will 
review 
and 
approve 
network 
development 
plans. 
As 
a 
planning, implementation 
and 
evaluation 
instrument, 
the 
PEI 
will 
become 
part 
of 
the 
routine 
school 
management 
process. 
To 
assist 
schools 
in 
implementing their 
PEIs, 
the School Development 
Fund 
will 
be 
established, 
which 
will 
channel 
resources 
to 
individual 
schools 
through their 
networks. 
Because 
of 
the 
newness 
of 
this 
process, 
implementation 
will 
be gradual 
and 
it 
is 
expected that plans 
will 
be 
ready 
only 
during 
the 
second 
year 
of 
implementation. 
Thus, 
a 
total 
of 
150 
PEIs 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
financed: 
fifty 
in 
the 
second 
year, 
and 
one 
hundred 
in 
the 
third 
year. 
The 
amount 
available 
for 
each 
project 
will 
be 
defined 
based 
on 
the 
size 
and 
characteristics 
of 
four 
types 
of 
school networks. 
The 
MED 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
monitoring 
and 
evaluating the 
process, 
the 
use 
of 
resources, 
and results. 
- 
54 
- 
Page  61
Financing. 
The 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
individual 
grants 
(school development 
fund) 
to 
support 
PEIs 
implementation. 
3.4 
National 
Assessment 
System 
(US$4.61 
million) 
Objective. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
help 
the 
Quality 
Assessment 
Unit 
(Unidad 
de 
Medicibn 
de 
la 
Calidud Educativa 
- 
UMC) to 
carry 
out 
activities 
to 
continue strengthening 
its 
technical capacity 
and 
promoting 
the 
enhancement 
of 
an 
"assessment 
culture" throughout the 
entire 
education 
system 
and 
the 
strengthening 
of 
the 
Education Statistics 
Unit 
(Unidad 
de 
Estadistica Educativa 
- 
UEE). 
Zmplementution 
arrangements. 
MED 
has 
two 
key 
units 
responsible 
for following 
up 
progress 
in 
the 
sector's 
performance: 
(a) 
The 
first 
one, 
the 
UMC, 
has 
significantly 
increased 
its 
technical capacity over 
the 
last eight 
years 
and 
has 
conducted three 
sample-based 
national 
assessments 
in 
1996, 1998 and 2001. 
The 
first 
two 
assessments 
were norm-based 
and 
were 
not 
calibrated 
so 
as 
to 
allow 
the 
monitoring 
of 
results 
over 
time. 
The 
results 
from 
these 
assessments 
were 
published 
only 
recently, 
due 
to 
the 
previous 
government's concerns about 
making 
them public. The 2001 
assessment was 
criterion-referenced 
and, 
for 
the 
first 
time, sampled schools 
in 
rural 
areas, 
including 
Quechua 
and 
Aymara 
speakers. 
This 
assessment 
will 
provide 
the 
baseline 
measures 
of 
student 
performance 
for 
the project/program; 
and 
(b) 
The 
second 
area, 
the 
UEE, 
is 
responsible 
for 
the  development  and 
follow-up 
of 
information 
and 
has 
evolved 
significantly 
in 
tracking 
enrollment 
and 
internal  efficiency 
indicators.  However, 
it 
is 
seriously 
constrained 
by 
low 
capacity 
at 
the 
local 
levels 
for 
collecting 
and  processing 
information. 
Data 
dissemination 
is 
mainly 
limited 
to 
the 
availability 
of 
large 
databases 
in 
MED's 
website 
which 
are 
difficult 
to 
manage. 
UEE 
will 
provide 
the 
Project 
with 
the 
necessary 
information 
to 
build 
indicators. 
It 
will 
also 
assist 
in 
the 
final 
identification 
of 
the 
geographical 
limits 
to 
the networks 
through 
the 
GIS 
system. 
The 
sub-component 
will 
help 
UMC 
to: 
(a) 
develop 
and 
administer 
the 
2004

f$omacibn 
ciudadana)
