the project 
areas. 
3.5 
Strategic 
Analysis 
and Policy-oriented Research 
(US$2.82 
million) 
Objective. 
The 
purpose 
of 
this 
sub-component 
is 
to 
support 
system 
capacity 
to 
expand 
and 
deepen 
its 
knowledge 
of 
meaningful school 
and 
learning variables. 
Implementation 
arrangements. 
Studies 
will 
be 
carried 
out  under 
MED 
Rural  Education 
Office 
coordination 
in 
the 
overall 
perspective 
of 
this 
Project 
and program. Selection 
of 
the 
studies 
will 
be made 
within 
four 
large 
groups 
of 
themes: 
(a) 
teacher 
career development, 
(b) 
decentralization and school 
autonomy, 
(c) 
development 
of 
strategies 
to 
provide 
demand 
incentives; 
(d) 
introduction 
of 
the 
intercultural 
component 
in 
the 
classroom. 
Financing. 
The 
sub-component 
will 
finance: 
(a) 
consulting 
services, 
(b) 
occasional traveling, 
and 
(c) 
printing 
of 
the 
research documents. 
3.6 
Project Management 
(US$2.35 
million) 
Objective. 
The 
purpose 
of 
this 
sub-component 
is 
to 
support 
project 
implementation, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation. 
Implementation  arrangements. 
The 
PCU 
will 
be 
established 
as 
part 
of 
MEDs 
Strategic 
Planning 
Secretariat and 
will 
operate 
according 
to 
the 
structure and 
management 
procedures 
set 
forth 
in 
the 
Project 
Operations 
Manual 
(OM). 
The 
PCU 
will 
be guided 
and 
monitored 
by 
the 
Project 
Steering 
Committee. 
Approval 
of 
the 
OM 
and establishment 
of 
the 
Project 
Steering Committee 
are 
conditions 
for 
Project 
Effectiveness. The 
PCU 
will 
be responsible 
for 
coordinating 
and 
monitoring 
the 
yearly Operations 
Plan, 
financial 
management 
of 
the 
project, 
and 
procuring 
goods 
and 
services. 
Financing. 
The 
sub-component 
will 
finance operating 
costs 
including: 
(a) 
office 
equipment 
and 
related 
maintenance 
of 
the PCU; 
(b) 
refurbishment 
of 
office 
space 
and 
office 
supplies 
of 
the PCU; 
(c) 
travel 
and 
per 
diem 
of 
MED 
personnel 
for 
Project 
implementation 
purposes; 
(d) 
salaries 
of 
PCU 
staff;  and 
(e) 
Project 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation.

Annex 
4: 
Cost 
Benefit Analysis Summary 
PERU: 
Rural Education 
Project 
Introduction 
A 
cost 
benefit 
analysis 
was 
carried out 
in 
two 
parts: 
initial 
education 
intervention 
(Component 
1) 
and 
the 
primary education 
intervention 
(Components 
2 
and 
3). 
The 
secondary distance 
education 
sub-component 
was 
not 
subject 
to 
this 
analysis 
because 
it 
is 
designed 
as 
a 
pilot 
specifically 
to 
gather 
information 
for 
future economic 
analysis and 
final 
component 
design. 
The 
cost 
benefit 
analysis 
is 
based 
on 
economic 
rates 
of 
return 
calculated 
under 
alternative 
scenarios 
of 
changes 
in 
efficiency 
rates. 
Different 
combinations 
of 
paths 
in 
the 
reduction 
of 
dropout 
and 
repetition 
rates are consistent 
with 
different 
values 
of 
completion 
rates 
in 
2013. 
This 
Annex 
summarizes 
the 
cost 
benefit 
analysis. 
The main 
document 
is 
included 
in 
the 
Project 
files. 
Overall 
Results 
The results 
of 
the 
cost 
benefit 
analysis 
are 
based 
on 
relatively 
conservative 
and 
alternative 
scenarios 
that 
were 
constructed 
using 
various 
assumptions 
of 
improvements 
in 
efficiency 
rates, 
including 
productivity, 
repetition 
and 
dropout 
rates 
resulting 
from 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Project. 
To 
find 
the 
economic 
return 
of 
the Project, 
each 
scenario 
is 
compared 
to 
a baseline 
(without 
project) that 
assumes 
a 
10 
percent 
reduction 
of 
repetition 
and dropout 
rates 
for 
primary 
and 
secondary 
levels. 
The 
analysis 
shows 
that 
the 
project 
is 
reasonably 
viable 
from 
an 
economic 
point 
of 
view. Despite 
being 
conservative 
in 
terms 
of 
the 
expected 
improvement 
in 
external 
efficiency 
rates 
and 
with 
conservative 
assumptions 
of 
GDP 
growth, 
ERR 
(Economic 
Rate 
of 
Return) 
estimates 
fall 
in 
the 
range 
of 
8 
percent 
to 
16 percent 
for 
the 
initial 
education intervention. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
primary 
education 
(Component 
2 
and 
3-, 
the estimated 
ERR 
fall 
in 
the 
range 
of 
8 
percent 
to 
23 
percent. 
In 
a 
reasonable 
scenario, 
in 
which primary 
completion 
rates reach 
90 
percent 
in 
10 
years, 
the 
ERR 
ranges 
between 
11.4 
percent 
and 
17.7 
percent, 
depending 
on 
assumptions 
regarding 
once 
and 
for 
all 
small 
increases 
in 
productivity 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
improvement 
in 
the 
quality 
of 
education. 
The results 
of 
the 
analysis 
should be 
considered 
as 
a 
lower 
bound, 
given 
that the economic 
indicators 
used 
were 
quite 
conservative 
and 
the 
estimates 
of 
the 
ERR 
do 
not 
take 
into 
account 
all 
externalities 
resulting 
from 
the 
Project. 
In 
addition, 
several 
components 
of 
the project, 
such 
as 
the 
teacher 
training 
institutions 
accreditation 
system, 
the 
institutional 
strengthening and the 
reform 
of 
the 
teachers' career 
are 
elements 
that 
will 
benefit 
all 
Peruvian 
students. 
Thus, 
the analysis leads 
to 
the 
conclusion that  there 
is 
reasonable 
certainty that a 
comprehensive 
intervention 
in 
rural 
education, 
such 
as 
this 
Project, should 
effect 
changes 
in 
key education indicators, 
as 
long 
as 
it 
is 
implemented appropriately 
and 
as 
planned. 
Gains 
from 
the 
project 
are 
observed 
much later 
in 
time, 
but 
are 
crucial 
in 
terms 
of 
improvements on the 
economic 
and social 
well-being 
of 
beneficiaries. 
Summary 
of 
Benefits 
and 
Costs: 
costs 
The 
costs 
were 
calculated 
for 
the 
initial 
education 
intervention 
(Component 
1) 
and 
the 
primary 
education 
intervention 
(Components 
2 
and 
3) 
including 
direct 
and 
recurrent 
costs 
of 
the 
project, 
and the 
opportunity 
cost 
of 
going 
to 
school, 
as 
children 
join 
later 
the 
labor 
market 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
lower 
dropout 
rates. 
- 
58 
- 
Page  65
The 
opportunity 
cost 
was 
considered 
as 
a 
key component 
of 
the 
cost analysis. 
More 
time 
spent 
in 
school 
implies 
working 
less, 
which 
is 
particularly important 
in 
Peruvian 
rural 
areas. 
However, the market value 
of 
that 
time 
is 
low, 
as 
most 
children 
will 
work 
at 
home 
as 
unpaid 
family 
workers. 
In 
spite 
of 
this, 
in 
terms 
of 
value, 
the 
opportunity 
cost 
is 
one 
of 
the 
largest cost 
components. 
Some 
important 
issues 
should 
be 
taken 
into 
account: 
(i) 
only 
additional 
current 
costs 
due 
to 
the 
increase 
in 
the 
enrollment 
in 
primary 
and secondary 
education 
are 
considered 
in 
the 
evaluation; 
(ii) 
infrastructure 
investments 
that 
might 
be 
required 
due 
to 
an 
increase 
in 
enrollment 
were 
not 
considered; 
(iii) 
the 
model 
assumes 
that 
students 
who dropout 
from 
the 
school 
system 
start 
working 
the 
following 
year, 
except 
for 
children 
under 
10 
who do 
not 
work, 
because 
the 
labor 
market 
is 
too small 
and, 
therefore the 
opportunity 
cost 
for 
these 
children 
is 
negligible; 
and 
(iv) 
there 
are 
additional 
costs, 
for 
example, 
the 
additional 
cost 
incurred 
by 
MOE 
and 
parents 
as 
children 
stay 
longer 
in 
school. 
The 
following 
costs 
were 
used 
in 
determining the 
additional 
costs 
to 
the 
GOP: 
Component 
1: 
Expanding Education 
Access 
for 
Rural Children 
US$ 
1.4 
million 
of 
training 
costs 
should 
be 
added together 
with 
US$ 
1.6 
million 
of 
rehabilitation 
of 
infrastructure 
to 
the 
US$2.7 
million 
allocated 
to 
this 
component. 
During 
the 
period 
2003 
- 
2005, 
the 
salaries 
of 
new 
teachers 
and 
“animadoras” 
amount 
to 
approximately 
US$4.3 
million 
in 
the 
first 
three 
years 
of 
the project. 
Additional 
recurrent 
costs 
in 
respect 
to 
salaries, 
training 
and 
maintenance 
starting 
in 
2007 
are 
calculated 
to 
be 
US$ 
3.3 
million, 
although 
they 
fall 
slightly 
afterwards. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
the interventions 
for  children 
0 
through 
2 
years 
old,  the 
GOP 
is 
expected 
to 
continue 
paying 
a 
stipend 
to 
animadoras 
of 
US$43 
per month, 
and 
also 
salaries 
of 
US$ 
171 
to 
one teacher 
per 
each 
10 
animadoras. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
the 
children 
3 
to 
5 
years 
old, 
the 
same 
salaries 
for 
animadoras 
and 
teachers 
are 
considered. 
Materials, 
monitoring 
activities,  computer 
replacement, etc  are also 
part 
of 
recurrent 
costs, 
comprising 
US$0.4 
million 
per 
year. 
Component 
2: 
Improving Quality 
in 
Rural 
Primary 
Schools 
a) 
Teacher 
Training 
System: 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
Training 
costs 
remain 
constant 
during 
the 
first 
three 
years 
and 
after 
that 
amount 
to half 
of 
what 
was 
spent 
during 
the 
investment period. 
Computers 
are 
replaced 
every 
four 
years. 
Furniture 
is 
replaced 
every 
ten 
years. 
Training 
materials 
are 
replaced every 
year. 
Monitoring 
costs 
are 
incurred 
every 
year. 
Overall, recurrent 
costs are 
high 
in 
2006 
and 
2007 
and 
then 
stay 
at 
US$ 
4.8 
million 
per 
year, 
mainly 
due 
to 
training. 
b) 
Provision 
of 
Educational 
Materials 
Sub-component 
0 
0 
0 
Workbooks 
will 
be 
replaced 
every 
year. 
Texts 
and 
instructional 
guides 
will 
be replaced every 
2 
years 
. 
Recurrent 
costs are 
about 
US$ 
1.4 
million, 
depending on 
the 
timing 
of 
replacement 
of 
text 
and 
workbooks 
and other 
materials. 
- 
59 
- 
Page  66
c) 
Rehabilitation 
and 
Equipping 
of 
rural 
schools 
0 
0 
0 
Annual 
infrastructure 
maintenance 
costs 
are 
US$ 
100 
per 
classroom. 
Computers 
are 
replaced every 
four 
years. 
Furniture 
is 
replaced every ten 
years. 
Component 
3: 
Reform 
of 
Teacher 
Policy 
and 
Education 
Management 
In 
this 
case, 
recurrent 
costs are based 
on 
the 
cost 
of 
implementing 
standardized 
tests 
periodically 
and 
computer replacement every 
four 
years. 
This 
amount 
is 
approximately 
US$0.4 
million. 
The 
cost 
analysis 
assumes 
that 
household 
expenditures 
per 
pupil 
will 
remain 
constant over 
time 
at 
US$22, US$27, US$90, 
for initial, 
primary 
and 
secondary 
students 
respectively. 
Public 
expenditures 
are 
also considered 
and 
are assumed 
to 
remain 
constant 
per 
pupil 
at 
the 
2002 
levels, 
i.e. 
US$164 
and 
US$235 
for 
primary 
and 
secondary 
education  respectively. 
Cost 
per 
pupil, 
however, 
may 
increase 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
the 
teachers' career 
reform. 
Only 
current 
additional 
costs due 
to 
increase 
in 
enrollment 
in 
primary 
and secondary are 
considered 
in 
the evaluation. 
Costs 
exclude 
taxes. 
Benefits 
The 
measurable 
benefits 
of 
the 
whole project 
stem 
from 
the 
reduction 
in 
repetition 
rates, 
which 
lead 
to 
savings 
in 
terms 
of 
overall 
expenditures per 
pupil. 
As 
quality 
is 
improved, 
productivity 
associated 
to 
education 
rises 
and students 
stay 
longer 
in 
school. 
As 
average years 
of 
schooling 
increase, 
so 
do future 
earnings 
to 
beneficiaries. 
The 
children 
of 
the 
290 
school networks 
in 
11 
departments 
are 
direct 
beneficiaries 
of 
the 
project, 
and 
they 
are 
considered 
as 
unique beneficiaries 
in 
the 
economic 
assessment. 
However, 
several 
interventions 
generate 
new policies, knowledge and educational technologies that 
will 
benefit 
all 
children 
in 
rural 
areas, 
and 
in 
some 
cases, 
the 
whole educational 
system. 
This 
also leads 
to 
an 
underestimation 
of 
the 
benefits. 
The 
methodology 
for 
the 
calculation 
of 
the 
benefits 
of 
the 
project 
includes: 
a) 
calculation 
of 
the 
flow 
of 
students 
from 
grade 
to 
grade 
in 
order 
to 
determine: 
(i) 
the 
expected 
number 
of 
students 
who complete 
primary 
level 
at 
any 
age; 
and, 
(ii) 
the number 
of 
students 
who 
enter 
the 
labor 
market 
having 
acquired 
a 
specific schooling level; 
b) 
various 
assumptions 
are 
made 
for 
the 
reduction 
of 
repetition 
and 
dropout 
rates, 
which 
lead 
to 
different 
enrollment  rate 
scenarios. 
Based 
on 
these 
assumptions,  changes 
in 
completion 
rates 
and 
schooling attainment 
due 
to 
increases 
in 
efficiency 
of 
the 
system 
are 
calculated; 
and, 
c) 
increases 
in 
schooling attainment 
were 
converted 
to 
monetary 
values 
using 
earnings 
predictions 
based 
upon coefficients  estimated 
from 
an 
earnings 
function 
which 
contains 
yearly 
labor 
income, 
as 
depended 
variable, 
and 
as 
independent 
variables: 
sex, 
potential 
experience, 
hours  worked per 
year, 
dummy 
variables 
for 
educational levels (complete 
secondary 
and 
incomplete 
and 
complete), 
years 
in 
primary 
and 
secondary. 
The 
benefit Project analysis 
of 
the 
took 
into 
account 
the 
following: 
Component 
I: 
Expanding Education 
Access 
for 
Rural 
Children. 
The 
beneficiaries 
of 
this 
component 
will 
be: 
(i) 
30,000 
children 
0-2 
years 
old with 
the 
implementation 
of 
a 
non-formal 
approach 
in 
which 
children 
will 
be 
educated 
along 
with 
their 
parents and siblings 
within 
a 
family 
community 
framework, 
and 
(ii) 
a 
total 
of 
38,000 
children 
3-5 
year 
old 
are 
expected 
to 
benefit 
through 
an 
intervention 
combining 
of 
formal 
and 
non-formal 
approaches. 
For 
the 
calculation 
of 
the 
benefits, 
it 
was  assumed 
that 
the 
number 
of 
-60- 
Page  67
entrants 
to primary 
school 
each year 
is 
the 
same 
as 
the 
number 
of 
5 
year 
old 
students 
enrolled 
the 
year 
before on 
a 
CEI 
or 
PRONEI. 
Component 
2 
and 
3:lmproving 
Quality 
in  Rural 
Primary 
Schools, 
Reform 
of 
Teacher 
Policy 
and 
Education 
Management. 
This 
component 
will 
benefit 
215,000 
rural 
primary 
education 
students 
in 
290 
networks 
in 
11 
departments. 
The 
first 
step 
in 
the 
calculation 
of 
benefits 
is 
the 
analysis 
of 
the 
flow 
of 
students 
from 
grade 
to 
grade, 
through 
the 
estimation 
of 
a 
set 
of 
repetition, 
promotion 
and 
dropout 
rates 
for 
each grade. 
The 
number 
of 
new entrants remains constant 
after 
2005, 
when 
all 
the schools 
from 
the 
290 
networks have 
been 
intervened 
by 
the 
project. 
It 
is 
assumed 
that,  even 
if 
there 
is 
an 
expected 
demographic 
reduction 
in 
the 
size 
of 
the 
new cohorts 
in 
rural 
areas, 
this 
does 
not 
affect enrollments, 
since 
better school 
quality 
will 
tend 
to 
attract new 
students. 
Main 
Assumptions: 
General 
Issues 
As 
a 
consequence 
of 
a 
higher 
quality 
initial 
education, 
primary 
repetition 
and dropout 
rates 
are 
reduced. 
Estimations 
of 
economic 
rates 
of 
return 
are 
based 
on 
constant returns 
of 
education 
for 
each 
individual 
during 
the 
life 
cycle. 
However, 
the  analysis 
assumes  scenarios 
of 
increased 
productivity. 
Private 
net 
benefits 
are 
negative 
during 
the 
first 
years 
due 
to 
opportunity 
costs. 
It 
is 
important 
to 
note that reductions 
in 
dropout 
rates 
reduce 
returns 
due 
to 
the 
weight 
of 
opportunity 
costs, 
as 
a 
result, 
future 
returns 
due 
to 
more 
years 
of 
schooling 
are 
highly 
discounted. 
Regarding 
direct 
costs, 
the 
first 
three 
years are 
pre-tax investments and recurrent 
costs. 
Additional 
household 
expenditures 
remain 
constant since 
2015. 
It 
may 
be 
argued that 
given that 
the 
public 
sector 
will 
provide 
rural 
students 
with 
more learning 
materials, 
household 
will 
not 
need 
to 
devote 
resources 
to 
education 
as 
they 
currently 
do. 
However, 
it 
could 
be 
possible that 
a 
higher 
quality 
education 
is 
perceived 
as 
a 
more 
profitable 
investment, 
so 
families 
are 
willing 
to 
devote 
more 
resources. 
In 
this 
case, 
it 
was 
considered that 
family 
expenditure 
per student 
remain 
at 
their 
2000 
level. 
Additional public 
costs 
due 
to 
enrollments 
depend 
on 
per 
student 
public 
expenditures. 
These 
expenditures 
are 
assumed 
to 
stay 
constant 
at 
their 
2002 
level. 
It 
is 
known 
that, 
if 
educational 
expenditures 
increase 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
a 
larger educational 
effort 
at 
the macro level, 
or 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
growth,  expenditures 
will 
be 
greater. 
However, 
assuming any increase 
in 
the 
educational 
system, 
expenditures 
per 
student 
should 
imply 
a 
change 
in 
the 
quality 
of 
the service, 
which 
should have 
an 
impact over 
benefits. 
Therefore, 
in 
order 
to 
isolate 
these 
effects 
of 
the 
project, 
it 
is 
reasonable 
to 
assume 
that 
there 
are 
no 
additional 
changes 
in 
the 
educational 
system.

Financial 
Analysis: 
This 
section 
presents 
recent data 
regarding 
educational expenditures 
in 
Peru, 
several 
alternative 
scenarios 
of 
expenditures 
for 
the 
following 
years, 
and the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
costs 
over 
total 
expenditures. 
There 
are 
only preliminary 
figures 
for overall 
educational expenditures 
in 
2003. 
As 
shown 
in 
Table 
1, 
between 
2000 and 
2001 
there 
was 
a 
reduction 
in 
educational 
expenditures. 
During 
the 
first 
half 
of 
2001, 
with 
the 
Transition 
Government, there 
was 
a 
contraction 
of 
fiscal 
expenditures. Later, 
in 
the 
second 
half 
of 
the 
year, 
the 
new 
government increased 
expenditures 
slightly, 
including 
a 
9 
percent raise 
in 
teachers 
salaries, 
which 
was 
the 
first 
increase 
in 
pensionable 
salaries 
in 
25 
years. 
In 
2002, 
public 
educational 
expenditures were 
US$ 
1.7 
1 
billion, 
representing 
3 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
- 
63 
- 
Page  70
Given 
the 
priorities 
established 
by 
the 
current administration, 
it 
is 
likely 
that 
resources 
devoted 
to 
education 
will 
increase. 
The GOP 
has 
established, 
although 
not 
formally, 
two 
specific 
targets, 
one 
is 
the 
increase 
of 
the 
share 
of 
education 
in 
the 
public 
budget 
from 
the 
current 19 percent 
to 
30 
percent 
in 
2006. 
The 
second one 
is 
doubling 
teachers’  salaries 
by 
that 
year. 
It 
will 
probably 
be 
difficult 
to 
meet 
any 
of 
these 
targets. 
In 
what 
follows, 
scenarios 
that contemplate 
the 
implications 
of 
meeting 
these 
targets 
are 
analyzed. 
Table 
3 
shows 
a 
projection 
for 
the 
period 
2004-2009 assuming 
that expenditures 
in 
education reach 
30 
percent 
of 
the 
public 
budget 
in 
2006. 
Given 
the 
paucity 
of 
the 
increase 
in 
fiscal

and 
the 
deficit 
targets 
set 
by 
the 
GOP, 
it 
is 
unlikely 
that 
public 
expenditures 
as 
a percentage 
of 
GDP 
will 
increase. 
In 
that 
case, 
and assuming 
therefore 
that 
non

stays 
at 
15.5 
percent, 
reaching 
this 
target 
is 
consistent 
with

4.65 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
2006. 
Attaining 
this 
target 
would 
imply 
increasing educational expenditures 
by 
75 
percent. 
Per 
capita 
public 
expenditures 
as 
a 
percentage 
of 
per capita 
GDP, 
a 
measure 
of 
the “educational 
effort“ 
of 
the 
country 
would 
reach 
14.2 
percent 
in 
primary 
and 
20

in

up 
from 
8.2 
and 
11.6

in

Many 
of

student 
are 
near

For

of

average 
4.8

3.2

3.7 
percent. 
Still,

What

way

14

14

14

22 percent.

public

.

15

Fiscal 
Impact: 
The 
fiscal 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
is 
relatively 
small. 
We 
have calculated the 
state 
counterpart, 
the 
implicit 
additional 
fiscal 
costs 
due 
to 
higher  enrollments 
generated 
by 
both 
the 
initial 
education 
and basic 
education interventions, recurrent 
costs and 
debt 
service. 
The 
financial impact 
has 
been 
calculated on the 
basis 
of 
the 
third 
simulation 
of 
overall fiscal 
expenditures 
and 
using 
the 
scenario 
of 
the 
economic 
analysis, 
which 
assumes 
a 
reduction 
in 
repetition 
rates 
of 
50 
percent and dropout 
rates 
of 
75 
percent 
by 
2009. 
Until 
2009, 
all 
implicit, 
recurrent 
and 
direct 
expenditures 
of 
the 
project 
amount 
for 
less 
than 
1 
percent 
of 
the 
overall 
expenditures 
in 
education, 
reaching 
at 
most 
0.78 
percent 
of 
additional 
costs. 
Also, 
they 
represent less 
than 
2 
percent 
of 
current 
expenditures 
in 
initial 
and basic education. 
Recurrent 
costs are 
mainly training 
expenditures 
and 
materials. They 
increase 
sharply 
in 
the 
two 
years 
following 
phase 
one 
due 
to training 
interventions that 
start 
in 
2004 and 2005 
that last 
for 
three 
years. 
Afterwards, 
these 
costs 
fall.

16.89 
million

Experience 
with 
small works 
in 
the 
rural 
areas 
of 
Peru 
has 
shown that 
procurement 
through 
contractors 
has 
not 
been successful 
mainly 
because 
of 
the 
relative small 
contract 
amounts 
(typically 
less 
than 
US$ 
60,000) 
for 
distant locations 
where 
transportation 
costs are 
made 
more expensive 
due 
to 
poor 
road 
quality. 
An 
alternative 
previously 
used 
by 
Bank 
projects 
has 
been 
the 
community contracting 
through 
the Peru’s 
Social Investment 
Fund, 
FONCODES, 
and 
it 
is 
the 
proposed 
strategy 
for 
implementation 
of 
works under 
this 
project. 
Below 
is 
a summary 
of 
the 
FONCODES project 
cycle. 
The  Operations 
Manual 
will 
include 
a 
detailed description 
of 
the 
Project 
Cycle to 
be 
implemented under 
this 
project. 
FONCODES 
has 
developed a 
project 
cycle which 
involves 
direct 
community  contracting 
which 
has 
proven 
to 
be 
quite 
successful 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
rural 
works 
and 
which 
has 
been 
reviewed 
in 
detail 
by 
the 
Bank 
as 
part 
of 
the 
implementation 
of 
two 
prior 
projects (FONCODES and 
FONCODES 
11). 
The 
project cycle 
was 
reviewed 
under 
the 
preparation 
of 
this 
project 
and 
also 
under the preparation 
of 
an 
IDB 
financed 
loan 
(FONCODES 
III) 
in 
order 
to 
introduce 
sustainability 
measures 
at 
the 
onset 
of 
the 
project 
cycle. 
The project 
cycle 
starts 
when the 
community 
elects (through 
at 
least 
two 
thirds 
representation) the 
Nucleo 
Ejecutor 
which 
has 
four 
members 
(President, 
Treasurer, 
Secretary and a 
Fiscal).  The 
Nucleo 
-71 
- 
Page  78
Ejecutor, 
as 
legal 
representative 
of 
the 
community, 
signs 
the 
project 
agreement 
with 
FONCODES.  The 
Nucleo 
Ejecutor 
is 
responsible 
for 
executing 
the 
project, 
while 
FONCODES 
takes 
on the responsibility 
for 
the 
design, 
evaluation 
and 
supervision 
of 
the 
works. 
FONCODES 
will 
disburse 
to 
the 
Nucleo 
Ejecutor’s 
bank 
account 
60% 
of 
the 
total 
subproject 
cost. 
Further 
disbursements 
will 
be 
made 
upon 
submission 
of 
expenditure reports 
and 
approval 
by 
the supervisor. 
In 
order 
to 
execute the 
works 
the 
Nucleo Ejecutor 
will 
hire, 
with 
FONCODES 
assistance, 
the 
Resident 
Engineer, 
who 
will 
provide 
the 
participating 
community 
with 
technical 
and 
administrative knowledge 
in 
order 
to 
implement 
the 
work. 
The 
Nucleo 
Ejecutor 
will 
procure 
the 
goods and 
services, 
with 
the 
advice 
of 
the 
Resident 
Engineer 
following 
Price Comparison (shopping) 
procedures. 
FONCODES 
will 
designate 
a 
Project Supervisor 
who 
will 
be 
in 
close 
coordination 
with 
the 
Nucleo 
Ejecutor 
and 
the 
Resident 
Engineer 
during 
the 
execution. Furthermore, 
the 
MED 
will 
have oversight 
authority 
to 
ensure 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
works.

10.02. 
The

and 
FONCODES 
have 
in 
place

management 
systems 
that 
can 
provide,

of

Disbursement 
Mechanism

of

of

(SOEs):

certified

of 
expenditures 
(SOEs).

will

and

for

by 
Bank 
supervision missions 
and independent

Additional 
Annex 
11: 
STRATEGY TO 
INCLUDE INDIGENOUS 
COMMUNITIES 
IN 
THE 
RURAL 
EDUCATION 
PROJECT 
PERU: 
Rural Education 
Project 
I. 
BACKGROUND 
1. 
LEGAL FRAMEWORK 
The Peruvian 
legal 
framework includes 
the 
right 
of 
indigenous 
peoples 
to 
education 
in 
various 
legal 
instances. 
Peruvian norms 
in 
this 
respect 
are 
included 
in 
the 
political 
constitution 
of 
the 
nation, 
the 
General Education Act, 
the 
Primary 
Education Regulations 
and the recent 
regulations creating 
in 
the 
National 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education Directorate 
and 
its 
Consultative 
Council. 
Article 
2, 
of 
Peru’s 
Political 
Constitution 
mentions 
that 
all 
individuals 
have 
the 
right 
to 
preserve 
their 
ethnic 
and 
cultural identity; 
that 
the Peruvian 
state 
recognizes and 
protects ethnic and 
cultural 
plurality 
in 
the 
nation; 
that 
bilingual 
and 
inter-cultural 
education 
must 
be 
fostered, 
recognizing 
each  area’s 
characteristics 
while 
preserving 
the 
various 
cultural 
and 
language 
manifestations. 
Peru 
has 
signed 
ILO’S 
169 
Agreement 
(ratified 
in 
1993) 
recognizing 
the 
right 
of 
indigenous boys 
and 
girls 
to 
learn 
to 
read and 
write 
in 
their 
own 
language, 
and preserve 
and develop 
it. 
It 
also recognizes the 
right 
of 
indigenous 
peoples 
to 
be 
consulted 
about 
state measures 
aimed 
at 
accomplishing 
these 
goals. 
Likewise, 
Peru 
has 
signed 
the 
Universal Declaration 
of 
Human 
Rights, 
article 
26 
of 
which 
establishes 
that  education 
will 
have 
as 
its 
objective  the 
achievement 
of 
full 
individual 
development,  and 
to 
strengthen 
respect 
for 
human 
rights 
and 
fundamental 
freedoms 
while 
fostering 
understanding, 
tolerance 
and 
friendship 
among 
all 
nations 
and 
ethnic or 
religious 
groups. 
Peru 
has 
also 
signed the 
Convention 
on 
the 
Rights 
of 
Children, 
article 
29 
of 
which 
mentions that children’s education 
must 
be 
destined 
to 
create 
in 
them 
respect 
for 
their 
own 
cultural identity, their 
language and values 
while 
preparing 
them 
to 
lead 
a 
responsible 
life 
in 
a 
free 
society, 
where 
the 
spirit 
of 
understanding, 
peace, 
tolerance, 
gender 
equality, and 
friendship 
among 
peoples, 
ethnic, 
national 
and 
religious 
groups 
and 
peoples 
of 
indigenous roots 
will 
be 
preserved. 
The 
General 
Education 
Act, 
in 
article 
4 
(d), 
specifies that 
preferential 
attention 
will 
be 
given 
to 
marginalized 
sectors, 
border 
areas, 
rural 
areas 
and 
population 
groups where 
native 
languages 
and other 
similar 
situations 
are 
predominant. 
Article 
5 
(c) 
mentions that  cooperation 
and 
exchange 
with 
other 
cultures, 
in 
particular 
the 
culture 
of 
other 
Latin 
American 
nations, 
will 
be 
fostered 
while 
preserving 
national 
independence 
and 
identity, 
and 
fostering the 
study 
of 
those 
nations’ 
living 
languages. 
To 
achieve the 
above 
goals, 
the 
new 
Primary 
Education Regulations 
crate the 
means 
to 
provide 
Andean 
and 
Amazonian communities 
with 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
schools, 
with 
education 
provided 
in 
the 
indigenous mother tongue 
and Spanish 
progressively 
taught 
as 
a 
second language, 
thus 
helping to 
build 
among 
the 
students 
a stronger language and 
cultural identity 
in 
the framework 
of 
the 
nation’s 
life. 
On 
April 
2, 
2001, 
Supreme 
Decree 
No. 
018-2001-ED 
created 
the 
National 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
Directorate 
(DINEBI in 
Spanish) 
to 
provide quality 
solutions 
to 
meet 
the educational 
needs 
and 
demands 
of 
a 
multicultural 
and 
multilingual 
nation, 
such 
as 
Peru, 
at 
all 
levels 
and 
for 
all 
types 
of 
education. 
On 
May 
22 
of 
the 
same 
year, 
Ministerial 
Resolution 235-2001-ED 
created 
the 
National 
Consultative Committee 
to 
promote 
greater 
involvement 
of 
civil 
society and 
direct 
beneficiaries 
in 
Page  95
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education. 
This 
committee includes outstanding 
representatives 
of 
the 
teaching 
profession 
from 
the 
Aguaruna, 
Shipibo, 
AshBninka, 
Aymara 
and 
Quechua nations, 
as 
well 
as 
Peruvian 
experts 
in 
anthropology, education 
and 
linguistics. 
Recruitment 
of 
bilingual 
teachers 
is 
regulated 
by 
Directive 
002-2002-CNCP-ED 
according 
to 
which 
teachers 
will 
be 
appointed 
to 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
schools 
only 
if 
they  have demonstrated 
written 
fluency 
in 
the 
respective indigenous 
language. 
In 
all 
cases, 
the 
selection 
criterion 
will 
be 
the 
relationship 
between the 
students  and 
their 
language. 
The  Selection Committee that 
recruits 
teachers, 
include 
a 
representative 
of 
the 
local 
indigenous 
federation. 
In 
addition, 
Vice 
Ministerial 
Resolution 
025-96-ED 
determines that 
to 
be 
hired 
as 
bilingual 
educators, 
teachers 
must 
be trained 
in 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education, 
talk 
the mother 
language spoken 
by 
the 
local 
children 
and 
be 
fully 
identified 
and 
committed 
to 
the 
children 
and 
community 
where 
they 
will 
work. 
2. 
ETHNIC 
AND 
CULTURAL CONTEXT 
2.1 
Our 
peoples 
The Peruvian 
nation 
is 
made 
up 
of 
a 
range 
of 
ethnic, 
cultural 
and language groups. Peru’s 
population 
- 
estimated 
at 
about 
24 
million 
- 
is 
mostly mixed. 
More 
than 
8 
million 
Peruvians 
belong 
to 
indigenous 
nations, 
mostly 
Quechua 
and 
Aymara 
living 
in 
the Andean region. The Peruvian Amazon forest 
covers 
62 
percent 
of 
the 
territory 
and 
is 
home 
to 
42  ethnolinguistic 
groups 
whose 
cultural, 
economic and 
political 
characteristics 
are 
obviously 
different 
from 
those 
of 
the 
national 
population. 
Such 
cultural 
variety 
spans 
factors 
such 
as 
culture, 
lifestyles, 
language, 
demographics, 
relation 
to 
the 
territory 
and 
contact 
andor 
interaction 
with 
society 
at 
large 
and 
the 
national 
juridical 
order. 
The 
universe 
of 
indigenous 
peoples 
is 
not 
restricted 
to 
peasant 
and 
native 
communities. 
It 
also 
includes 
remote 
settlements, 
and 
groups 
that 
have 
voluntarily 
decided 
to 
isolate 
themselves 
or 
who 
only 
establish 
sporadic contact 
with 
the 
rest 
of 
the 
country.  There 
is 
an 
undetermined number 
of 
individuals 
of 
indigenous roots 
living 
in 
Amazon 
cities 
or 
rural 
areas, 
who 
are 
known 
as 
the 
riverside 
population. 
A 
greater 
recognition 
is 
required 
of 
the 
rights 
of 
those peoples 
who 
do 
not 
explicitly 
identify 
themselves 
as 
native, indigenous nations or 
members 
of 
a 
given 
language 
group, 
other than the 
1265 
peasant 
and 
native 
communities 
identified 
and 
registered 
by 
the 
Peruvian 
government. 
2.2 
Our 
languages 
Peru’s 
official 
languages 
are 
Spanish, 
as 
well 
as 
Quechua, 
Aymara and Amazon 
languages 
in 
locations 
where 
they 
are 
predominant. 
Quechua 
is 
spoken 
by 
4 
million 
people 
while 
there 
are 
some 
350,000 
Aymara 
speakers. 
In 
the Amazon 
region 
between 
300,000 
and 
400,000 
people 
speak 
any 
of 
the 
40 
Amazonian 
languages 
grouped 
in 
16  language 
families, 
i.e. 
Arabela,  Arahuaca,  Bora,  Chafil-pana, 
Candoshi-Zarpa, 
Harakmbut, 
Huitoto, 
Jibaro, 
Pano, 
Peba-yagua, 
Simaco, 
Tacana, 
Ticuana,  Tucano, 
Tupi-Guarani 
and 
Zaparo. 
Some 
100,000 
people 
speak 
a 
foreign 
language. 
Tables 
la 
and 
lb 
show 
the 
population 
sizes 
for 
each 
Andean and Amazonian 
language 
respectively. 
Table 
2 
shows 
the languages 
to 
be 
covered 
in 
the 
Rural 
Education Project. 
- 
89 
-

Page  98
3. 
EXIECUTIVE SUMMARY 
OF 
THE 
BENEFICIARIES’ 
ASSESSMENT 
The 
beneficiaries’ 
assessment 
comprised 
two 
stages. 
During 
the 
first 
stage, 
running 
from 
the 
second 
semester 
1999 
to 
April 
2000, 
was 
carried 
out 
by 
the 
Bilingual 
Inter-cultural 
Education 
Training 
Program 
(PROEIB 
Andes 
in 
Spanish) based 
in 
the 
city 
of 
Cochabamba, 
Bolivia. 
During 
this 
period, 
visits 
to 
bilingual 
schools 
in 
the 
departments 
of 
Puno, 
Cuzco, 
Ancash, 
San 
Martin, 
and 
Amazonas 
provided 
information 
about 
teacher and student 
performance. 
In 
addition, 
bilingual 
training 
was 
evaluated 
during 
a 
workshop 
with 
teacher trainers. 
The 
activity 
brought 
together 
approximately 
120 
teachers 
from 
various 
Andean 
and 
Amazonian  regions 
around 
Peru. 
The  opinions 
of 
parents, 
teachers 
and 
training 
organizations (executing 
agents) 
were 
another 
source 
of 
information. 
From March 
to 
May 
2001, 
the 
second 
stage 
of 
the 
evaluation 
focused on 
the 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
policy 
guidelines, 
and 
the 
text  books 
and 
materials prepared 
by 
the former 
UNEBI, 
now 
renamed 
as 
DINEBI. 
The 
recommendations 
from 
this 
evaluation 
have 
been 
used 
in 
the 
design and 
proposed implementation 
of 
the 
Project. 
Peru 
has 
undergone 
an 
education 
reform 
that 
introduced 
a 
more active 
and 
horizontal 
teaching 
approach 
called 
the 
New 
EducationaE 
Approach. 
Some 
changes 
were 
readily 
observed 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
evaluation. 
3.1 
Teaching practice 
Findings 
and 
results 
from 
these 
two 
assessment stages 
include 
the 
following: 
Findings 
concerning: 
the 
students’ 
organization 
and 
classroom design 
Teachers 
organize 
classrooms 
in 
a 
less 
rigid 
manner 
than 
the 
traditional 
face-to-face 
approach. 
Even 
when 
classroom 
furniture 
made 
it 
difficult 
to 
rearrange the 
students 
around 
the 
classroom, 
these 
were 
redesigned 
to 
give 
an 
emphasis 
to 
and 
facilitate 
teamwork. 
Very 
few 
classrooms were 
found 
where 
students were 
organized 
in 
the 
traditional row-and-line 
organization. However, and 
despite 
the 
ongoing 
changes, 
most 
teachers 
failed to 
move around 
the 
classes 
and 
rather 
chose 
to 
teach 
their 
classes 
from 
the 
front 
of 
the 
room 
and 
with 
their 
backs 
to 
the blackboard. 
Findings 
concerning the 
relationshiu 
between 
teachers 
and 
students and the 
uarticiuation 
of 
bow 
and 
gi& 
The 
classrooms 
environment 
is 
more 
open and 
horizontal, 
and 
fosters 
increased 
involvement 
of 
boys and 
girls. 
Additionally, 
the 
use 
of 
the 
students’ 
mother tongue gives 
students 
more security 
and 
confidence. 
However 
it 
is 
also 
important 
to 
acknowledge that 
greater 
involvement 
depended 
to 
a 
large extent 
on 
the 
permanent 
stimulus 
and 
direction  provided 
by 
the 
teacher. 
Additionally, 
some 
teachers 
did 
show 
discriminating 
and 
physically 
abusive 
behavior towards children. 
Findings 
concerning classroom 
work 
styles 
Teachers 
still 
almost 
totally control 
education 
processes 
in 
the classroom and 
give 
instructions 
in 
a 
top-down 
style 
with 
few 
opportunities 
for 
reflection 
and 
even 
less 
for 
metacongnition. 
Routine 
actions 
and 
schemes 
evidence 
the 
control 
and 
domination  functions 
exerted 
by 
teachers. 
The 
most  salient 
classwork 
aspects 
are 
described 
below: 
- 
92 
- 
Page  99
Teaching 
Materials 
Diversification 
of 
teaching materials 
is 
still 
incipient. 
In 
many 
instances, 
the 
only 
materials 
used 
in 
the 
classroom 
are 
chalk 
and a blackboard, 
except 
in 
some 
cases 
when 
the 
teacher 
uses 
flip 
charts and markers 
while 
students 
do 
teamwork. 
Also, 
in 
a 
significant 
number 
of 
classrooms, 
posters 
and 
illustrations 
prepared 
by 
teachers 
and 
students 
decorate 
the 
walls. 
Most 
teachers 
appreciated 
the 
workbooks 
prepared 
and 
distributed 
by 
UNEBI, 
although 
in 
some 
cases 
they 
were 
not 
yet 
using 
them. 
Most 
survey 
respondents 
mentioned that workbooks 
were 
slow 
to 
reach 
them. 
The 
most 
widely 
used 
by 
both 
teachers 
and 
students 
were 
for 
logic 
and 
mathematics, 
while 
comprehensive 
communication workbooks 
were 
little 
used 
because 
of 
the 
language 
barrier. 
Use 
of 
languages 
Major 
changes 
were 
observed 
concerning 
the 
use 
of 
languages 
in 
the 
classrooms. 
Generally 
speaking, 
in 
the 
surveyed 
area, 
when the 
students 
spoke 
predominantly 
a 
vernacular 
language, 
classwork 
was 
done 
in 
that 
language, 
whether 
Aymara, 
Quechua 
or 
Aguaruna. 
Class 
sessions 
generally 
were conducted 
in 
the 
vernacular 
language and 
in 
Spanish, 
alternatively. 
In 
the Andean 
area, 
the 
simultaneous 
use 
of 
two 
languages 
in 
the classroom apparently 
depends 
on 
the 
teacher’s 
decision and 
also 
possibly on 
the 
parents’ pressure 
to 
use Spanish. 
This 
simultaneous 
use 
of 
the 
two 
languages 
is 
disorganized 
and, 
in 
some 
cases, 
requires translating 
or 
rewording. 
Also 
this 
situation 
seems 
to 
result 
from 
insufficient work 
materials 
in 
Spanish 
as 
a 
second language, 
and 
little 
methodological 
training 
for 
teachers 
so 
that  they 
can 
clearly 
establish 
the 
difference 
between 
teaching 
a language and 
teaching 
in 
a 
given 
language. 
Although 
the 
vernacular 
language 
is 
used 
in 
teaching, 
there 
is 
no 
thinking 
about 
it, 
nor 
is 
its 
conscious 
and 
reflective 
use 
promoted beyond 
simply learning 
to 
write 
it. 
Still 
introducing 
vernacular 
languages 
in 
study programs 
seems 
to 
have created, 
albeit 
in 
a 
limited 
manner, 
an awareness 
of 
their 
value. 
Inter-cultural 
dimension 
in 
the 
classroom 
Teacher  have 
multiple difficulties 
when 
trying 
to 
introduce 
the 
multicultural 
dimension 
in 
a 
practical 
way. 
Although 
teachers 
may have 
been 
trained 
in 
the 
theoretical 
and 
conceptual 
aspects, 
there 
is 
a 
manifest 
need 
for 
specific teaching guidelines 
to 
introduce 
a 
more horizontal 
communication 
approach 
and 
enforce 
inter-cultural 
teaching 
in 
everyday 
classroom work. 
Nevertheless, 
some teachers 
already 
use 
local 
knowledge 
and wisdom, 
although 
incipiently. 
Evaluation 
Evaluation 
is 
one 
of 
the 
areas 
that 
has 
received the least attention 
as 
teachers 
or 
experts 
from 
teaching 
organizations 
do 
not 
include 
it 
in 
the regular teaching 
process. 
It 
has 
not 
yet 
become a 
continuing 
and 
permanent 
process, 
and 
traditional 
evaluation practices 
subsist. 
No 
cases 
of 
self-evaluation or 
peer 
evaluation 
were 
observed 
during 
the 
assessment. 
3.2. 
Boys 
and 
girls’ 
learning 
achievements 
A 
worrisome 
finding 
was 
that 
neither boys 
nor 
girls 
achieved the 
third 
level 
of 
reading 
and 
writing 
skills 
in 
the predominant 
language. 
In 
many 
instances, 
students were 
unable 
to 
read 
and 
write 
in 
the 
vernacular 
- 
93 
- 
Page  100
language. 
Similar, 
if 
not 
more 
severe 
issues, 
concern the 
use 
of 
Spanish 
as 
a 
second 
language. 
Although 
in 
many 
cases 
students 
were 
able 
to 
handle 
simple 
oral 
tasks 
related 
to 
basic 
social interaction, they had 
trouble 
describing 
a 
simple 
illustration 
using  complete 
and 
coherent 
phrases 
and 
sentences. 
Even 
greater 
difficulties 
were 
identified 
in 
reading 
comprehension and 
writing 
in 
the 
second language. 
Most 
students 
had trouble 
both 
reading 
and 
understanding 
a short 
academic 
piece. 
3.3 
Social and community 
participation 
Participation 
of 
parents and 
other 
members 
and 
leaders 
of 
the community 
at 
large 
in 
the 
education 
process 
in 
quite 
limited.  Their 
involvement 
is 
limited 
to 
maintenance 
or 
refurbishment 
of 
school 
facility 
and 
making additional 
financial 
contributions 
for 
specific 
purchases. 
More 
attention 
should be 
paid 
to 
this 
element 
to 
the extent 
social 
and 
community  involvement 
can have 
a 
major 
influence 
in 
creating 
acceptance 
for 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
(IBE) 
and 
determining 
the 
quality 
of 
IBE 
projects 
and 
programs. 
3.4 
Opinions 
on 
the 
program, 
its 
objectives and enforcement 
The 
following 
opinions 
were expressed 
by 
the various 
stakeholders 
concerning the 
programs’ 
objectives 
and enforcement: 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
4. 
Although 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
is 
widely 
accepted, teachers 
generally 
feel 
it 
is 
only 
a 
temporary 
way 
of 
using 
the 
Vernacular 
language 
in 
education 
during 
the 
students’ 
initial 
schooling 
and 
only 
until 
they 
have 
achieved 
sufficient 
mastery 
of 
Spanish. 
Teacher 
training 
time 
is 
insufficient 
considering 
all 
the 
components 
and 
tasks 
that 
need 
to 
be 
included. 
Training 
and 
on-site 
monitoring 
as 
well 
as 
the 
programs’ 
credibility 
are 
hurt 
by 
the 
trainers 
and 
specialist 
working 
in 
regional offices who do 
not 
speak 
the vernacular 
languages and 
lack 
experience 
in 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
(IBE). 
Parents 
and 
teachers 
must 
be 
further 
sensitized 
to 
IBE’s 
goals and 
objectives, and 
the 
advantages 
of 
using 
the 
students’ 
predominant 
language 
in 
education. 
Lack 
of 
information 
in 
this 
regard 
leads 
some 
to 
reject the 
IBE 
program. 
More 
coordination 
seems 
necessary 
among organizations 
charged 
with providing 
IBE 
training 
and 
regional 
and sub-regional specialists. 
For 
instance, 
teachers 
are 
relocated 
without 
due 
consideration 
to 
their 
IBE 
training. 
Not all 
IBE 
regional specialists 
speak 
the 
local 
vernacular 
language. 
In 
many 
cases, 
grade 
school 
experts were 
hired 
based 
on criteria 
that 
prioritized 
teaching 
in 
Spanish. 
Several respondents underscored the language component. 
Few 
referred 
to 
the 
cultural 
component, 
reflecting insufficient 
understanding 
about 
the 
inter-cultural 
nature 
of 
the 
IBE 
initiative. 
SUMMARY 
OF 
THE 
CONSULTATION 
PROCESS 
In 
order 
to 
include Inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
in 
the 
debate 
leading 
to 
the 
National 
Agreement 
on 
Education, 
a 
number 
of 
round 
tables and 
workshops examined the various 
experiences 
and 
contributions 
to 
the 
field 
of 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
in 
Peru and the region. 
This 
consultation 
process 
comprised 
several 
stages 
and 
involved 
various 
government 
and 
nongovernmental 
organizations, 
universities, 
inter-cultural 
and 
bilingual 
experts. 
Most 
importantly 
however, 
it 
included 
a 
substantial 
contribution 
by 
representatives 
from 
indigenous  and  native 
- 
94 
- 
Page  101
organizations, 
as 
well 
as 
prominent 
bilingual 
teachers 
from 
various countries 
across 
the 
region. 
4.1 
First 
Consultation Event 
The 
first 
consultation 
process 
revolved 
around 
“Education 
and 
the Challenge 
of 
Diversity.” 
It 
involved 
participants 
from 
indigenous  organizations 
from 
the 
Andean 
and  Amazon 
regions, 
community 
federations,  associations, 
regional 
organizations, 
bilingual 
teachers, 
nongovemmental 
organizations, 
universities, 
inter-cultural 
and 
bilingual 
experts, 
a 
representative 
from 
the 
Congressional Education 
Commission, 
one 
from 
the 
Office 
of 
the 
Ombudsman 
and 
another 
from 
the 
Ministry 
for 
Women 
and 
Social Development. 
A 
working 
document 
on 
“Education  and 
challenges 
of 
diversity. 
Contributions 
from 
Zndigenous 
Peoples 
to the 
National 
Agreement 
on 
Education” 
was 
reviewed 
by 
the 
participants 
in 
the 
event. 
The 
modifications,  corrections 
and  suggestions 
are 
included 
in 
a 
publication 
called 
“The 
challenges 
of 
diversity.” 
In 
its 
first 
section, 
it 
includes 
a 
number 
of 
thoughts 
and 
proposals concerning diversity, 
exclusion, 
and 
education 
needs. 
In 
its 
second chapter, 
it 
proposes 
several actions 
to 
meet 
the 
challenge 
of 
such 
diversity. 
4.2 
Second 
consultation 
event 
The 
second 
consultation 
centered 
on 
the 
“Policies and 
Strategies 
for 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education.” 
It 
took 
place 
in 
May 
2001 
and 
had 
as 
main 
topic the 
debate 
on 
the 
policy 
guidelines 
of 
the 
Inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education. 
The 
working 
commissions discussed a 
number 
of 
policy 
guidelines 
in 
the 
following 
areas: 
Inter-cultural 
issues 
Curriculum 
Pre- and 
in- 
service 
teacher 
training 
Materials 
Research 
Preschool and 
primary 
education 
Secondary 
education 
Adult 
education 
Organization 
and 
management 
of 
IBE 
The indigenous 
groups represented 
in 
the 
commissions 
made 
a 
number 
of 
significant 
contributions. 
For 
instance, 
as 
a 
result 
of 
debate 
and 
consultations, the 
document 
on 
national 
language 
and 
culture 
policy 
on 
education 
was 
prepared, 
including 
general and 
specific 
policy 
guidelines 
to 
enhance 
the 
quality 
of 
education 
based 
on 
the 
principles 
of 
respect 
and 
acceptance 
of 
Peru’s 
cultural 
and language 
diversity. 
The 
policy 
proposing active and 
additive 
bilingualism 
as 
well 
as 
an 
inter-cultural 
approach 
as 
guiding 
principles 
of 
Peruvian education 
is 
on 
the way 
to 
becoming new 
Law 
of 
Languages 
and 
part 
of 
the 
new 
General Education 
Law. 
4.3. 
Organizations 
and representatives 
of 
Amazon and Andean 
peoples 
participating 
in 
the 
consultation 
During 
the

events, 
the

from

were 
actively

Martin 
-FEPIKRESAM. 
4.4 
IBE’s 
National 
Consultative Council 
Since 
its 
organization 
in 
May 
2001, 
the 
Council 
has 
prepared 
a 
final 
document 
on 
Peru’s 
National Policy 
on  Cultures 
in 
Education, 
and 
has 
proposed 
it 
as 
one 
of 
the  conceptual  underpinnings 
of 
Peru’s 
educational 
policy. 
4.5 
Consultations 
with 
SETAI 
The 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
National  Directorate 
actively 
participated 
in 
the 
consultation 
process 
promoted 
by 
the 
Technical 
Secretariat 
for 
Indigenous 
Affairs 
(SETAI) 
with 
support 
from 
the 
World 
Bank 
during 
the 
period 
1998 
- 
2000. 
Now, 
this 
technical 
secretariat 
reports 
to 
the 
National 
Commission 
for 
the 
Andean 
and 
Amazon 
Peoples 
presided 
by 
Peru’s 
First 
Lady, 
which, 
in 
turn, 
reports 
directly 
to 
the 
Prime 
Minister. 
11. 
STRATEGY COMPONENTS 
5. 
FUTURE CONSULTATION 
AND 
COMMUNITY PARTICIPATION 
PROGRAMS 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
has set 
itself 
the 
goal 
of 
strengthening a democratic 
culture 
in 
the Peruvian 
education 
system 
to 
create 
a more active 
and 
equitable citizenry. 
This 
implies improving 
the 
relationships 
between 
the 
state 
and the 
indigenous 
peoples, and 
also 
putting 
a number 
of 
resources 
into 
operation 
so 
that 
joint 
management 
and 
responsibility 
sharing 
in 
education 
will 
become a 
reality. 
Indigenous 
organizations, 
the 
educational community 
and 
civil 
society 
at 
large 
need 
to 
become 
actively 
involved 
in 
developing 
IBE 
proposals, 
in 
order 
to 
obtain 
active 
contribution 
from 
these 
organizations 
for 
the 
design, 
execution 
and 
evaluation 
of 
educational activities. 
This 
will 
be 
implemented 
by 
means 
of 
a 
consultation 
process 
that 
will 
also 
include 
all 
educational 
regional 
and 
provincial 
directorates. 
These 
initiatives 
will 
be 
complementary 
to 
the 
rural 
community 
participation 
strategies 
to 
meet 
the 
goals 
of 
improving 
and strengthening organizational 
management 
of 
education 
within 
schools 
with 
active 
involvement 
of 
parents, 
community 
members 
and 
officials 
through 
School 
Council, 
education networks, 
and 
all 
district, 
provincial 
and 
regional 
levels 
of 
government. 
Indigenous 
peoples 
will 
likewise 
become 
active 
at 
these 
levels 
of 
community participation. 
- 
96 
- 
Page  103
5.1 
Round Tables 
Between 
2004 and 
2007,28 
debate 
and 
consensus 
round 
tables, 
focusing 
on educational topics 
for 
each 
indigenous 
people, 
will 
be 
organized 
in 
the departments 
of 
Loreto,  Ucayali, 
San 
Martin, 
Amazonas, 
Puerto Maldonado, 
Junin-Pasco, 
Ancash, 
Apurimac, 
Ayacucho, 
Puno, 
Huancavelica, 
Madre 
de 
Dios, 
Lambayeque, 
Tacna, 
Moquegua 
and 
Arequipa. 
The 
round 
tables 
will 
focus 
on 
the relevance 
of 
language 
and 
cultural 
issues 
in 
education, 
with 
the 
purpose 
of 
proposing guidelines 
for 
the educational 
development 
plans 
for 
each 
of 
the 
participating 
indigenous 
peoples. 
They should 
also 
contribute 
to 
the 
diversification 
of 
the 
curriculum 
and 
matching 
student 
profiles 
to 
their 
respective cultures, 
languages, 
lifestyles 
and 
development 
approaches. 
Moreover, 
the 
round 
tables 
will 
perform 
a 
social  oversight 
function 
on 
the 
appropriate 
use 
of 
contracts 
and 
resources. 
5.2 
Regional Consultative 
Councils 
Regional 
Consultative  Councils 
will 
be 
promoted 
in 
educational  regions  and 
subregions 
with 
participation 
of 
representatives 
from 
organized  indigenous  communities, 
wherever 
appropriate. 
Indigenous 
peoples 
will 
be 
part 
of 
these 
councils  and 
will 
perform 
functions 
similar to 
those 
of 
the 
National 
Consultative 
Council 
of 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
established 
through 
Ministerial 
Resolution 
235-2001-ED. 
The 
Consultative Regional Councils 
will 
assess 
and evaluate the 
management 
of 
education, check 
the 
use 
of 
resources, and 
prepare 
educational 
policy 
guidelines 
for 
government 
regional 
educational 
officials, 
including 
recommendations 
and 
corrective 
actions 
for 
the 
Regional Education 
Directorates' 
operations. 
5.3 
National 
Congresses 
for 
Bilingual 
Education 
Teachers 
Annual 
meetings 
of 
bilingual 
teachers, 
nationwide, who excel 
in 
teaching 
and 
in 
their relationship 
with 
the  community 
will 
be 
promoted. 
The 
purpose 
of 
these 
meetings 
will 
be 
to 
exchange 
teaching 
experiences, 
promote 
the 
emergence 
of 
classwork 
innovation 
and 
assess 
the 
progress 
made 
in 
the 
field 
of 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
in 
Peru, 
as 
well 
as 
specific 
progress 
in 
various 
curricular 
areas. 
Four 
such 
national 
congresses 
for 
bilingual 
education 
teachers 
have 
already 
taken 
place, 
from 
1998 
to 
2001, 
in 
Junin, 
Ayacucho, 
Pun0 
and 
Cuzco. 
The 
fifth 
national  Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
Congress 
will 
take 
place 
in 
Lamas 
(San 
Martin) 
and 
will 
be organized 
by 
the Federation 
of 
Kechwa 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
from 
the 
San 
Martin 
Region 
- 
FEPIKRESAM. 
5.4 
Inter-Cultural 
Events 
for 
Students, Teachers and 
Parents 
Meetings 
and 
gathering 
of 
students, 
teachers 
and 
parents 
from 
Peru's  indigenous  populations,  the 
so-called 
Tinkuy 
jamborees, 
will 
be organized 
to 
improve 
inter-cultural 
skills 
for 
every 
day 
life. 
These 
meetings 
have 
taken 
place recently 
in 
the 
cities 
of 
Andahuaylas, 
Iquitos, Huanta, Huancayo and 
Pampas 
(Tayacaja, 
Huancavelica). 
At 
these 
meetings, 
participating 
students 
and 
teachers 
will 
have  the 
opportunity 
to 
demonstrate 
their 
teaching work. 
- 
97 
- 
Page  104
6. 
MATERIALS 
DESIGN 
AND 
TESTING 
Teaching materials 
for 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
are 
a substantive 
component 
of 
the 
strategy. 
From 
this 
viewpoint, 
initiatives 
are 
being 
considered 
for 
preparing 
and 
developing teaching materials 
for 
students, 
teachers, 
parents, and 
the 
community at 
large. 
6.1 
Student 
Materials 
One 
of 
DINEBI’s 
most 
important 
achievements 
has 
been 
to 
prepare and 
distribute 
educational 
material 
including 
workbooks 
for 
Language 
and 
Mathematics 
in 
the 
14. 
Andean  and  Amazon  vernacular 
languages. 
This 
material 
has 
been 
designed 
as 
a support and 
reinforcement 
for 
boys 
and 
girls’ 
leaming 
in 
these 
two 
key 
areas, 
in 
addition 
to 
social 
learning 
studies 
as 
well 
as 
science 
and 
environment. 
With 
respect 
to 
support 
material 
for 
second 
language, 
efforts 
are 
underway 
to 
prepare workbooks, posters and 
charts 
for 
the 
first 
two 
levels (cycle 
I) 
with 
a 
view 
at 
completing 
a 
series 
with 
advanced 
level 
materials 
(cycles 
11 
and 
III). 
After 
consultation 
with 
experts 
and recommendations 
from 
an 
independent evaluation, 
a 
validation 
process 
was 
launched 
for 
the Andean 
languages 
(Quechua and 
Aymara)  workbooks. 
This 
process 
will 
continue 
with 
the 
validation 
of 
materials prepared 
for 
the 
Amazonian 
languages. 
The testing tools 
are 
aimed 
at 
collecting 
data about the 
cultural, 
language and 
teaching relevance 
of 
the materials under 
review. 
The 
results 
from 
the 
validation 
will 
provide 
input 
to 
enhance 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
printed 
materials. 
Additionally, 
it 
is 
of 
utmost importance 
to 
start 
designing alternative materials 
so 
that the 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education proposal 
for 
multigrade  and  single 
teacher 
and  single 
teacher 
schools 
can 
be 
successfully tackled. 
DINEBI 
will 
prepare 
the educational materials modules 
for 
children 
in 
preschool 
and 
primary inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
using 
a 
self-paced methodology. 
These 
materials 
will 
be 
prepared 
using 
the vernacular 
languages 
as 
main 
languages 
of 
learning 
and 
in 
Spanish 
as 
the 
second 
language. 
The 
design 
process 
will 
adopt  a 
participatory 
approach 
involving 
regional 
teams 
and 
indigenous 
peoples. 
This 
dynamic 
approach 
will 
facilitate 
undertaking 
internal 
and 
external 
review 
processes, 
reprinting 
the materials 
and 
printing 
the 
final 
prototypes. 
Before 
they 
are 
delivered, 
these 
prototypes 
will 
be 
validated 
in 
the 
rural 
project 
intervention 
areas. 
DINEBI 
will 
prepare 
the 
educational material 
for 
students 
in 
a 
two-pronged 
strategy: 
A) 
Improving 
existing 
educational 
material 
This 
approach 
will 
consist 
in 
improving 
existing 
educational 
material 
prepared 
by 
DINEBI 
(workbooks) 
in 
14 
vernacular 
languages 
and 
in 
Spanish 
as 
a 
second 
language 
(Cycle 
I). 
DINEBI 
will 
also  start 
preparing complementary 
teaching, 
reading and 
writing 
material 
in 
vernacular 
languages 
as 
the 
main 
language 
of 
learning, 
and Spanish 
as 
a 
second 
language. 
This 
strategy 
will 
allow 
to 
cover 
85 
percent 
of 
demand 
by 
2005. 
Improving materials 
means: 
e 
e

6.2 
Teachers’ 
materials 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
that teaching 
of 
writing 
in 
indigenous 
languages 
is 
sustainable, 
Inter-cultural

will 
be

with 
a

manual that 
will 
give

the 
tools 
to 
better 
understand the 
writing 
and 
functioning 
of 
the 
main 
language 
used 
in 
classroom and instruction. 
These 
materials 
will 
support 
the 
distance 
teacher 
training initiative.

into 
account that 
classwork requires

ongoing

Materials 
for

Materials 
for

and 
the

at 
large, 
will 
be prepared 
in 
14 
languages 
as 
part 
of 
a

strategy 
to 
promote 
awareness 
on the

of 
IBE. 
These

will 
support

strategy 
and 
will

spots,

others. 
7. 
INTER-CULTURAL 
BILINGUAL 
TEACHER 
TRAINING 
Inter-cultural 
bilingual 
topics 
are 
included 
in 
the  in-service 
training 
initiative 
proposed 
by 
MED’s 
National 
Teacher 
Training 
Directorate (DINFOCAD). 
Teacher 
training 
schools 
and 
teacher 
training 
special projects 
will 
be 
restructured 
in 
order 
to 
meet 
future 
bilingual 
teachers’ 
needs, 
and 
to 
structure 
private 
and 
government 
initiatives 
so 
that 
both quality 
and 
better results 
are 
guaranteed. 
7.1 
IBE 
Teacher 
Training 
and 
Multiple 
Classrooms 
Schools 
As 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
education 
becomes 
stronger and 
more 
widespread, 
in-service 
teacher 
training 
will 
become 
increasingly important, 
as 
part 
of 
the Continuous 
Teacher 
Training 
System that 
will 
give 
rural 
teachers 
ongoing opportunities 
to 
update 
and 
improve their 
professional 
skills 
and 
performance. 
The 
system 
will 
aim 
at 
ensuring 
the appropriate educational human 
resources’ 
development 
as 
part 
of 
a 
coherent 
and 
uninterrupted 
training 
scheme 
to 
improve 
the 
quality 
and performance 
of 
teacher 
classwork 
and 
student 
learning.  Likewise, 
and 
taking

8. 
CURRICULUM 
DEVELOPMENT 
In 
coordination 
with 
the 
Initial 
and 
Primary 
Education 
National 
Directorate 
(DINEIE') 
and 
the 
Secondary 
and 
Vocational  Education  Directorate 
(DINESST), 
a 
curriculum 
proposal 
will 
be 
prepared 
and 
introduced 
that 
is 
related 
to 
the 
needs 
and 
demands 
identified 
among 
bilingual 
peoples. 
Likewise, further 
progress 
will 
be 
made 
in 
introducing 
an 
inter-cultural, 
bilingual 
and 
self-paced 
study 
program. 
As 
a 
complement, a 
contribution 
will 
be 
made 
to 
introduce 
further inter-cultural 
approaches and 
concepts 
in 
study programs 
at 
all 
levels 
and 
to 
promote the 
additional 
Spanish-native 
language 
bilingual 
learning 
approach 
so 
as 
to 
apply 
it 
through 
the 
whole  educational 
system 
and 
thus 
promote  dialogue 
and 
inter-cultural 
understanding 
across 
the 
nation. 
The 
development 
of 
Regional  and 
local 
guidelines 
will 
be promoted 
for 
the 
diversification 
of 
the 
curriculum 
from 
an 
inter-cultural viewpoint 
involving 
regional 
and 
sub-regional offices. 
To 
improve 
bilingual 
teacher 
classwork, a 
series 
of 
methodological 
strategies 
will 
be developed 
for 
each 
of 
the 
curriculum 
areas, 
with 
an 
emphasis 
on 
multigrade 
classrooms and 
for 
specific social and 
linguistic 
scenarios. 
The appropriate methodology 
handbooks 
will 
also 
be 
prepared. 
In 
order 
to 
adapt 
various 
inter-cultural 
bilingual 
learning 
to 
national 
bilingual 
contexts 
in 
Peru, 
a 
number 
of 
strategies 
will 
be developed 
for 
treating 
and 
using 
various 
languages 
in 
classroom situations, 
based 
on 
the 
cumulative 
experience 
gained 
to 
date 
in 
Peru 
and 
abroad.

RESEARCH

on 
the

and 
cultures 
of 
indigenous 
peoples 
will 
be promoted 
to 
cover the 
following

Ethnic 
numbering and neo-numbering 
systems: 
a study 
of 
the number 
systems 
in 
the 
Arawak,

Jibaro 
and 
Harakmbut 
language 
families 
to

customs and

DISSEMINATION 
A 
local 
dissemination 
strategy 
will 
be 
implemented 
in 
order 
to 
sensitize 
both 
indigenous 
and 
other 
peoples 
on the 
scope 
of 
the 
Inter-cultural 
Bilingual 
Education 
Program 
and 
its 
main 
components,  i.e. 
diversity, 
multiplicity 
of 
cultures, 
mutual 
respect, 
etc, 
a 
national 
and 
regional.  Radio 
and 
TV 
spots, 
posters, 
leaflets and 
bilingual 
information 
brochures 
will

as 
well 
as

site. 
11. 
ADAPTING 
SCHOOL 
SCHEDULES 
TO 
PEASANT AND 
INDIGENOUS 
RURAL 
ECONOMICS 
AND 
FARM 
WORK 
Rural 
Peru 
is 
a 
peculiar 
and 
complex 
world 
due 
to 
its 
geographic, social, 
cultural, 
economic 
and 
political 
diversity. 
All 
these 
must 
be 
taken 
into 
account 
in 
order 
to 
ensure 
access 
to 
and 
equal 
opportunity 
in 
education 
for rural 
boys 
and 
girls 
and 
provide them 
with 
quality 
education. 
A 
particularly important 
issue 
that 
needs 
to 
be 
taken 
into 
account 
in 
rural 
areas 
is 
the sewing-harvesting 
cycle. 
This 
cycle 
has 
a 
significant impact on 
student attendance 
during 
labor 
intensive 
periods. 
National 
Education 
regulations 
stipulate, 
that 
every 
school 
has 
the 
obligation to 
adopt 
to 
the 
local 
culture 
and 
environment. 
However 
schools have 
not 
implemented 
this 
mandate. 
This 
dissemination 
and 
promotion 
of 
this 
mandate 
will 
be carried out throughout 
the 
project 
areas. 
Additionally, 
from 
the 
viewpoint 
of 
curricular 
structure 
ad 
in 
coordination 
with 
DI", 
a 
self-paced 
approach 
to 
IBE 
will 
be 
implemented 
aimed 
at 
developing the 
students' 
potential. 
Rural children 
will 
gain 
access 
to 
a 
type 
of 
formal 
education 
that 
addresses 
their 
specific 
needs 
and 
contexts, 
that 
respects 
and 
assists 
in 
the 
development 
of 
their 
culture and 
language, 
and contributes 
to 
enhancing 
productive 
activities. 
As 
part 
of 
this 
model, 
grade 
promotion, 
as 
an 
indicator 
of 
the 
system's 
efficiency, 
will 
foster 
greater 
individual 
attention 
to 
monitor 
student 
achievement. 
To 
achieve 
this 
goal, 
the 
academic 
program 
will 
be 
organized 
as 
a 
chain 
of 
capacity 
building 
efforts 
centered 
on one 
area, 
and 
will 
be structured sequentially 
along six 
years 
of 
primary 
schooling 
and subsequently 
for 
high 
school 
education. 
Lastly, 
it 
is 
hereby proposed 
to diversify 
the 
school 
curriculum 
to 
include 
a 
number 
school calendar 
considerations and proposing 
backbone 
topics 
such 
as 
a 
multicultural 
approach, 
environmental education 
and 
production 
activities.

CRITERIA

The 
teacher

must 
ensure 
that the appropriate

and 
retained 
in 
order 
to 
provide quality 
and 
fair 
education. 
To 
do 
so, 
a 
periodical 
system 
for 
objective 
evaluation 
of 
teacher 
performance 
must 
be 
put 
in 
place 
so 
that: 
0

best 
teachers 
work 
in 
the 
public 
sector.

0

arbitrary. 
0

in

each 
school 
or network 
anywhere 
in

they 
need. 
Level 
The 
teacher 
screening 
and

is

in

to

Program. 
Basically,

new 
hiring 
system 
that 
reflects 
the 
government’s 
right 
to 
hire 
the 
best 
teachers 
and 
simultaneously gives 
teachers 
the 
right 
to 
job 
tenure 
on 
a merit-basis 
and 
subjected 
to 
performance 
evaluations.

network

in 
rural 
areas 
will 
be 
directly 
responsible 
for 
assigning teaching positions, 
and 
screening and 
hiring 
teachers.

of
